F.        Disbursement and Auditing

102.      Policy Support Program. The program will follow the Bank's disbursement procedures
for development policy credits. The untied budget support will be disbursed against satisfactory
implementation of the development policy program and not tied to any specific purchases;
therefore, there are no procurement requirements. Once the credit becomes effective, and the
conditions of disbursement have been met, the proceeds of the credit will be deposited by IDA in
an account at the Bank of Uganda at the request of the Borrower.

103.      The Borrower shall ensure that upon the deposit of the Credit into said account, an
equivalent amount is credited in the Borrower's budget management system, in a manner
acceptable to the Bank. The Borrower will report to the Bank on the amounts deposited in the
foreign currency account and credited to the budget management system. The administration of
this credit will be the responsibility of MEMD. See Annex 7 for detailed financial management
assessment.

104.      Project. A review of the financial management, auditing, and disbursement arrangements
for the Project has been conducted, and is described fully in Annex 7. Project auditing
arrangements have been agreed with the Borrower including internal and external audits as per
Bank requirements. Disbursements for the Project will be on the basis of Financial Monitoring
Reports, with which the project entities have satisfactory experience based on other Bank-
supported operations (see Annex 7 for detailed description).



4    The term "net revenues" means the difference between: (a) the sum of revenues from all sources related to operations, net
non-operating income and Government subsidies received towards thermal power costs or other operational costs; and (b) the
sum of all expenses related to operations including power purchase costs, "UMEME off-set payments," administration, adequate
maintenance, taxes and payments in lieu of taxes, but excluding provision for depreciation, other non-cash operating charges and
interest and other charges on debt. The term "UMEME off-set payments" means charges made by UMEME against UETCL for
losses incurred by UMEME in relation to the power crisis and in accordance with agreements reached between the two
companies.

                                  VI. APPRAISAL SUMMARY

A.      Economic and Financial Analyses

Economic Analysis (Project only; not required for Policy Support Program)

105.    The purpose of the economic analysis is to evaluate whether the short-term generation
facility whose costs will be partly defrayed by the proposed Credit, is an economic choice of
technology. Details of the demand forecast, thermal capacity costs, fuel costs, hydrologic data
and value of unserved energy are the same in this analysis as for the proposed Private Power
Generation (Bujagali) Project and are given in Annex 9.

106.    The urgency of the thermal investments is predicated more on the delay of the first
Bujagali project, combined with poor hydrologic conditions than it is on load growth, which has
been constrained by supply problems. As a result, the generation requirements forecast is
conservative and could well be exceeded.

107.    The feasibility of either importing firm electricity from the Kenya grid or locating a
Uganda-specific thermal plant in Kenya, in order to economize on fuel transportation costs, was
examined. Neither option is feasible within the required time period.

108.    The new short-term facility will be a high-speed diesel plant located in Uganda, burning
ADO. As it is a temporary, emergency facility, only technologies that can be: (a) mounted and
removed quickly; (b) run with fuels readily transportable to Uganda; and (c) viable over short
time periods were considered. This limits the choice to gas turbines and high speed diesel plants.

109.    The economic cost of using the short-term plant ranges between US$0.165 and US$0.423
per kWh (including a carbon emissions charge valued at US$15.3/Te of CO2) depending on the
fuel price, year and hydrology case. Details of the parameters and the calculations underlying
this estimate are contained in Annex 9.

110.    Benefit-Cost and Risk Analysis. The project benefit stream consists of system avoided
unserved energy (AUE) with the project versus the AUE without the project, multiplied by the
value of AUE (VAUE). The system AUE varies according to the hydrology, and as the
hydrology improves, the need for the project diminishes since more hydropower is available.

111.    VAUE is the Uganda-specific opportunity cost per consumer category of grid-based
electricity supply. For the commercial and industrial load it is the cost of self-generation, and for
the residential load it is the cost of using electricity substitutes. Calculations to determine these
values are shown in Annex 9.




                                                  29

112.     The marginal cost stream is the sum of system operating costs "with" versus "without"
the proposed project. The economic returns to the short-term plant are enhanced by low
hydrology, high demand and low fuel prices. Conversely they are reduced by high hydrology,
low demand and high oil prices. Hence the project's viability is at risk primarily from these three
factors. The Base, Low and High projections for each of these risk factors is assigned an
individual estimated probability of occurrence, allowing the calculation of a probability weighted
net present value (NPV) for each case and all of them in aggregate. Details of the sensitivity
analysis can be found in Annex 9.

113.     The analysis uses a merit-order dispatch model which calculates the AUE, VAUE, fully
allocated system-wide generation costs and net benefits for 45 different cases, and the overall
probability weighted NPV of the proposed investment. The results of running the model for all
45 cases at a 10 percent discount rate are shown in Table 6.

                                  Table 6: NPV of Short-Term Thermal Plant
                                            (All 45 Cases in US$ millions)

                                    End date
                                       for          Fuel Price Demand
    Plant Option                    simulation Fuel  Scenario Forecast            Hydrology Scenario
                                                                         1      2           3         4      5
    Mutundwe Thermal Power Station    2010     ADO    Base      Base   44.16  29.30       9.84      -23.37 -39.84
                                                                High   53.18  43.71      37.57       7.85  -36.03

114.     Light grey areas are positive outcomes and dark grey areas negative ones. As expected,
the proposed Project fares relatively better with lower hydrology, lower fuel prices, and higher
demand. The risk-inclusive NPV at 10 percent discount rate is positive at 1.2 million dollars, and
the inferred economic internal rate of return (EIRR) approximately 12 percent, with substantial
risk of more positive or negative outcomes depending on eventual demand, hydrology, and fuel
prices. The worst negative outcome is USD 42.3 million (probability of only 1.3 percent) and the
best positive outcome is USD 73.4 million (probability of only 0.5 percent). Out of the 45
scenarios, 21 cases are positive and 24 negative. These results should be viewed in the context of
the very conservative projections for demand growth and the VAUE, and the demonstrated
sensitivity of the results in respect of the demand forecast. Overall, this analysis indicates that the
expected economic outcome for this operation is satisfactory with an inferred EIRR of 12
percent, but with substantial downside and upside variations.




                                                            30

Financial Analysis (Policy Support Program and Project)

115.   In June 1999, the Government approved a comprehensive power sector reform strategy
focusing on improving efficiency through a structure that allows for a partnership between the
public and private sectors in the operation of the power system's assets and investment in the
system's development. Annex 1 provides an overview of the implementation of structural
reforms in the power sector. The consolidated performance of the generation, transmission and
distribution Ugandan power utilities is discussed here (Annex 9 provides more details on the
financial performance of the sector).

116.   Power Sector Performance (2004-06). The significant reduction in relatively
inexpensive hydropower supply over the recent past and in the medium term, has forced the
Government to implement an interim generation expansion plan (2005-10) to meet the country's
power generation needs until the proposed Private Power Generation (Bujagali) Project is
commissioned in early 2011. As already described under Section III above, this interim plan
includes the installation of up to 150 MW of thermal generation capacity (further details also in
Annex 1). This heavy reliance on very expensive thermal power combined with a reduced
amount of kWh energy to sell has significantly increased the sector's overall revenue
requirements as well as the average costs per kWh. Total revenue requirements in 2004 were
US$76 million, at which time the sector was financially viable. However, the corresponding
requirement in 2006 is approximately US$200 million, equivalent to an increase of 164% over
two years. Thus, electricity tariffs had not kept pace with rising costs since 2005, and the gap
between costs and revenues has widened considerably.

117.   Since the power crisis begun, the Government has shown its commitment to the power
sector by providing substantial support to UETCL (the single buyer in the power system) and in
spite of significant power cuts, ERA has also substantially raised tariffs (by a cumulative 151%
since April 2005), to meet the revenue requirements of the sector (together with budgetary and
donor support).

118.   The power sector's key operational and financial performance indicators for 2004-06 are
summarized in Table 7 below. The consolidated financial performance of Uganda Electricity
Generation Company Limited (UEGCL), Uganda Electricity Transmission Company Limited
(UETCL), and Uganda Electricity Distribution Company Limited (UEDCL) for 2004-06 and
projections for the period 2007-16 are provided in Annex 12.

119.    Electricity Tariffs. After the takeover of the distribution business by UMEME, tariffs
were increased effective April 1, 2005 by an average of 27%. This increase took account of
UMEME's revenue requirements and also the costs of the first 50MW thermal plant that was
commissioned in May 2005. Tariffs were increased subsequently by an average of 2%, 37.5%
and 41% on October 1, 2005, June 1, 2006, and November 1, 2006 respectively.

120.    Despite those significant tariff increases the domestic lifeline tariff for the first 15 kWh
per month was only increased one time in the last five years and less significantly than the
average retail tariff. The lifeline tariff was set at USh50/kWh (US$2.7¢/kWh) in 2001 and
represents approximately 5.5% of total consumption by end-use customers. In June 2006, the
life-line tariff was increased by 24% to USh62/kWh (US$3.4¢/kWh).




                                                         32

121.     Retail electricity tariffs are now below costs of supply. Today's weighted average retail
tariff excluding VAT is USh313/kWh (US$17.2¢/kWh and US$21¢/kWh including VAT). The
estimated actual average for 2006 was USh215/kWh (US$11.7¢/kWh). Based on accrued
revenue requirements, the required average tariff excluding VAT for 2006 was USh371/kWh
(US$20.2¢/kWh). The estimated subsidy for 2006 is around USh156/kWh (US$8.5¢/kWh),
equivalent to US$85 million for the calendar year. In 2006, the Government provided USh113
billion (US$62 million) in direct budget support towards thermal power costs of the power sector
and in the Government's Fiscal Year 2006/07 the total amount of subsidies will reach US$84
million under base case assumptions. In addition to the Government's support, UETCL was
directed by ERA to utilize the remaining balance of USh49 billion (US$27 million)5 in the Bulk
Supply Tariff stabilization fund that had been collected from electricity customers in prior years.
This fund was fully utilized in the first quarter of 2006.

122.     Uncollected Energy Bills. Another important factor of Uganda's power sector is the
amount of losses and uncollected energy bills. Since its take over of the distribution concession
from the state owned UEDCL, the collection rate had already been improved by UMEME until
May 2006 from 80% up to 92%, but since the large increases in tariffs in June and November
2006, the collection rate dropped again to 82% at the end of 2006. Equally the transmission and
distribution losses remained at levels of about 4.5-5.0% (transmission losses of UETCL) and
34.1% (distribution losses of UMEME) since mid-2006. This means that at the end of 2006
approximately 49% of the energy sent out is not paid for. It will be crucial that loss numbers and
collection rates improve again. UMEME is actively pursuing various measures to accelerate
technical and non technical loss reduction and to improve collection rates. The distribution
concession has recently been renegotiated between UMEME and the Government due to the
current electricity crisis. The restructured concession agreement includes commercial incentives
for the concessionaire to reduce losses and non-collection rates.

123.     Sector Revenue Requirements and Indicative Financing Plan (2007-2011). A
summary of the base case forecast of the power sector revenue requirements and an indicative
financing plan for 2007-11 is provided Annex 9, as well as the annual sector revenue
requirements and the financing plan for 2007-16.

124.     Total revenue requirements of the power sector for 2007-11 are estimated at US$1,495
million. Based on the tariff levels effective from November 2006, the power sector will raise
revenues of US$1,147 million in that same period, leaving a shortfall of US$348 million during
2007-11. In addition to this shortfall in 2007-2011 the sector also has to service a remaining
shortfall of USh 8 billion (approx. US$ 5 million) that was carried forward from the previous
years.



5Note that US$4 million out of the US$27 million have been applied to outstanding revenue shortfalls carried forward from the
previous calendar year 2005.




                                                            33

125.   It envisages the following revenue raising measures:

   · Deferment of USh128 billion (US$67 million) of debt service by GOU to 2011; the
       sector should be in a position to repay all of the deferred debt service by the end of 2011
       and will be expected to meet all of its debt service obligations to GOU from 2011
       onwards;

   · USh92 billion (US$49 million) annually in 2007 and 2008, USh28 billion (US$15
       million) in 2009, and USh66 billion (US$34 million) in 2010. The slight increase of GOU
       support in 2010 compared to the previous year is due to the fact that the Project will no
       longer provide funds for the financing of the energy charges of the power station
       supported under the Project. On this basis, the projected direct GOU budget support
       applied towards tariffs is estimated to reach USh278 billion (US$147 million),
       representing 10% of sector revenue requirements from 2007 to 2011. No Government
       support will be needed beyond 2010.

   · IDA support (under the Project hereto) of US$ 206.5 million towards the capacity and
       energy charges of a 50MW thermal plant. The financial assistance will comprise US$42.1
       million towards capacity payments and US$164.4 million towards energy charges,
       including fuel.

126.   Electricity Tariff Increases: According to the World Bank's forecast, international
prices of crude oil are expected to decline gradually over the coming years, to reach around
US$45 per barrel by 2011 (in 2006 prices). Based on this oil price forecast, and after taking
account of Government and IDA support towards thermal power costs, the financial analysis
indicates that there will be no need for further revisions in electricity tariffs through to 2011.
This means that tariffs will fall in real terms over the course of the next five years from the
present US$17.2¢/kWh to US$13.8¢/kWh by 2011. However, if crude oil prices from today to
2011 would slightly increase to US$73 per barrel (in 2006 prices), the projections show that an
additional amount of US$92 million would have to be raised through additional tariff increases
or additional tariff subsidies.

127.   Figure 1 depicts the "glide path" of the average electricity tariff until 2011. The revenue
requirements above the dark line show the amount of "tariff subsidy" being provided by the
Government through direct budget support, debt service deferment and IDA support towards
thermal costs. The projected revenue requirements and tariffs converge by the time the proposed
Private Power Generation (Bujagali) project is commissioned in 2011. Electricity tariffs would
be fully cost reflective by then and subsidies would be removed, except for duty exemptions on
generation fuel and transmission investments.

130.       Despite the fact that only 5% of Uganda's population has access to electricity and that
only a very low level of power consumption is attributed to the poor (see Section E below), the
proposed temporary subsidization of the electricity tariffs is justified for two main reasons:

     (i)         As Box 1 in Section II above shows, the expansion of the industrial and commercial
                 sector plays an important role for Uganda's future economic growth prospects. The
                 recent CEM for Uganda points out that the expansion of the commercial and
                 industrial sector is dependent on the provision of reliable and affordable power supply
                 for daily production purposes. The current fully cost-reflective end user tariff would
                 have to be around US$ 0.26/kWh. Such a tariff level would have negative
                 implications on the opening of new businesses and thereby on the expansion of the
                 industrial and commercial sectors. Temporary GOU subsidies are necessary to
                 support economic growth as long as the power crisis exists.

     (ii)        Another reason that justifies the current GOU support to the power sector is the
                 necessary expansion of the customer base and improvement in access to electricity.
                 Fully cost reflective tariffs would seriously hinder or even stop completely
                 UMEME's ability to expand its customer base as unsubsidized tariffs would be
                 unaffordable for the potential new residential and non residential customers. GOU
                 support therefore can also be justified by the important need to increase access to
                 electricity.


6Note that this table shows expenses per calendar year. Government's fiscal year is from July 1 ­ June 30.




                                                                    36

131.   However, as the table above also shows, there will not be a need in the assumed Base
Case for Government subsidies after the commissioning of the Bujagali hydropower plant, which
confirms the temporary nature of the proposed GOU financial support. Subsidies will no longer
be required as the Bujagali Dam commissioning will allow for the immediate displacement of at
least 738 GWh of diesel generation under Base Case hydrology assumptions.

B.     Technical (Project only; not required for Policy Support Program)

132.   The technical aspects of the Project are standard, straightforward, and non-controversial.
Diesel engine technology is well-known and widely used for short-term operations such as
proposed for support under this project. Interconnections of the short-term plants with the
national grid will be/are undertaken in compliance with accepted engineering practice and
supervised by UETCL's capable staff. Likewise, the energy efficiency measures contemplated
are well-known and widely used.

C.     Fiduciary

133.   Policy Support Program and Project. The Country Financial Accountability
Assessment (CFAA) carried out by IDA in 2004 shows that GOU has made substantial progress
in improving its public financial management systems since the last CFAA undertaken in 2001.
However, risks remain in terms of enforcement of procurement and payroll rules and procedures;
completeness of data on debt; effective independent oversight, and timeliness and effectiveness
of legislative and public scrutiny. As a result of the CFAA, GOU has come up with a strategy to
mitigate these country level issues, by designing a Financial Management and Accountability
Program (FINMAP). FINMAP's objective is to address the CFAA action plan issues formulated
within the Country Integrated Fiduciary Assessment (CIFA) and improve on Uganda's Public
Financial Management­Performance Measurement Framework indicators developed by the
Public Expenditure and Financial Accountability Secretariat. FINMAP is to be supported by a
number of Development Partners, including the Bank, which form the Public Financial
Management Donor Group.

134.   Project. The Project's transactions will be managed within the existing set-up in UETCL
and MEMD. The Manager, Finance, Accounts & Sales of UETCL and the Project Accountant of
MEMD will be in charge of maintaining the accounts and records of the components of the
Project that they are implementing. Both MEMD and UETCL have Financial Management
Manuals in place. The Project's financial statements will be audited in accordance with statutory
requirements, and suitable Terms of Reference will be developed.

135.   Actions outlined in the Financial Management Action Plan will be undertaken by the
Project to strengthen the financial management system. One action to be completed within six
months after credit effectiveness is reaching agreement on the terms of reference and




                                                 37

appointment of the external auditor, to ensure that Project accounts are audited and submitted to
the Bank in a timely manner.

136.   In order to ensure that the Project is effectively implemented, UETCL and MEMD will
ensure that appropriate staffing arrangements are maintained throughout the life of the Project.

137.   The conclusion of the assessment is that the financial management arrangements have an
overall moderate risk rating for both MEMD and UETCL, which satisfies the Bank's minimum
requirements under OP/BP10.02 and therefore the implementing agencies are adequate to
provide, with reasonable assurance, accurate and timely information on the status of the project
required by IDA. With the implementation of the Action Plan, the financial management
arrangements will be strengthened.

D.     Procurement

138.   Policy Support Program and Project. In Uganda, all procurements utilizing public
funds are governed by the Public Procurement and Disposal of Public Assets Act, 2003 (the
Procurement Act). The Procurement Act, based on the UNCITRAL model law, established a
regulatory body, the Public Procurement and Disposal of Public Assets Authority (PPDA).

139.   The 2004 Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR) indicates that the
Procurement Act represents effective and suitable legislation. However, there are a few issues
with the law and regulations that should be addressed. These issues, which are described in
Annex 8, are more appropriately addressed through the ongoing PRSC support.

140.   Project. Procurement for the proposed Project will be carried out in accordance with the
World Bank's "Guidelines: Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits," dated May 2004
(revised October 2006); "Guidelines: Selection and Employment of Consultants by World Bank
Borrowers," dated May 2004 (revised October 2006); and the provisions stipulated in the
Financing and Project Agreements. For each contract to be financed by the Credit, the
procurement methods or consultant selection methods, the need for pre-qualification, estimated
costs, prior review requirements, and time frame have been agreed between the Borrower and the
Bank and are outlined in the Procurement Plan. See Annex 8 for a description of procurement
arrangements.

E. Poverty and Social Impacts

Policy Support Program

141.     Since only about 5 percent of Uganda's population has access to electricity, any
additional power generation, as well as any improvements in reliability, will certainly provide
substantial benefits to the country and to poor households in particular. In general, the benefits to
economic growth are expected to come from the multiplier effects of providing a reliable supply
of electricity, which is a fundamental requirement for promoting new investments. In addition,
even communities that have no household electricity connections will benefit when the energy
program expands, as basic services become more accessible. As the sector becomes self-
sustaining and revenue-generating, allowing the power sector reforms to take effect, more
resources will be freed up and reallocated for education, health, and agriculture. Consequently,
as these services expand, including through synchronization with energy programs, the country's
fulfillment of the Millennium Development Goals will be facilitated.

142.     In general, effects of changes in electricity tariffs on the poor can be determined through
impacts on affordability and willingness-to-pay. In Uganda, however, the current electricity
coverage in poor areas is much smaller relative to wealthier areas and affordability becomes less
of a concern for three reasons. First, access to electricity is very limited at this stage so the major
poverty implications of energy policies would be through increasing generation and improving
transmission and distribution to rural, poor households.

143.     Second, contrary to what may generally be found in many other countries, wealthier
households in Uganda spend more on electricity.7 The top 10 percent of households spend 12.5
percent of their total expenditures on energy compared to 1.3 percent among the poorest 10
percent of the population. Changes in electricity tariffs are anticipated to have minimal direct
effects on household consumption and on poorer families' electricity consumption. In addition,
Uganda has a Lifeline tariff that is available to users that consume less than 15 kWh per month
has remained stable as noted above.8 Nevertheless, to better understand the implications of these
changes among families who are currently receiving grid-connected electricity, a poverty and
social impact analysis (PSIA) will be undertaken (see below). The results of the PSIA will be
used to identify measures to better protect poorer households from short-term increases in
electricity tariffs.

144.     Third, the Rural Electrification Strategy and Plan of 2001 is currently implementing a
poverty targeted energy program. It aims to provide a connection rate of 10 percent of rural
households by 2012, or about 400,000 new connections, most of which will be in poor villages.


7   Electricity Regulatory Authority, Study on Social and Economic Impact of Electricity Tariff Changes, Report submitted by
Department of Social Work and Social Administration, Makerere University, 2004.
8   More details on the affordability for residential consumers of the historic and planned tariff increases can be found in the
Bujagali II: Economic and Financial Evaluation Study, Report submitted by Power Planning Associates Ltd, February 2007.




                                                             39

According to the country's poverty mapping, the rural energy subsidies will be prioritized
according to districts where the majority of the extreme poor reside: districts with less than 20
percent of population living below the poverty line; districts with 20-35 percent; and districts
with more than 35 percent below the poverty line. The proposed energy subsidy will cover local
distribution of grid-connected generation projects; mini-grids around isolated generation plants;
grid-connected distribution extensions with no generation of their own; and small, stand-alone
systems with one or few consumers (e.g., photovoltaics or PVs; small diesel).9

145.     The Project provides an allocation for technical assistance to the GOU to lead a group of
local consultants in conducting the PSIA of the ongoing policy reforms in the energy sector. The
PSIA will: (a) examine the distributional implications of electricity tariffs and expanded rural
electrification; (b) assess the poverty impacts of a reallocation of expenditures from energy to
sectors like education, health, and agriculture; (c) address sector policy related environmental
impacts; and (d) recommend actions for Government and other stakeholders to ensure that both
distributional and environmental impacts are taken into account in the implementation of power
sector policies and programs. The matrix in Annex 10 summarizes the key issues.

Project

146.     The investments in thermal power generation and energy efficiency are expected to have
overall positive social and environmental benefits, ultimately leading to increased economic
activities, more employment opportunities, and improved delivery of education, health, and other
services. Further, alternatives to burning fuelwood and charcoal in woodstoves would not only
reduce deforestation but also improve health, especially among women and children, as a result
of reduced indoor air pollution. Aside from their positive environmental impacts, investments in
energy saving technologies, for example in buildings and industrial sites, such as use of more
energy efficient light bulbs, will promote behavioral change and increase awareness of benefits
from using these technologies.

147.     There are no negative social impacts to energy efficiency programs. However, in the co-
financing of the thermal power generation plant in Mutundwe, two possible social impacts were
identified. One is the acquisition of a one acre property. A Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) for
this property was completed, consistent with Government and World Bank requirements. The
RAP covered the survey of the land, appropriate compensation, and informed consultation with
affected people.




9Rural Electrification Agency, REA Subsidy Policy Document, 2006.




                                                          40

F.      Environment

Policy Support Program

148.    Energy policy reforms are generally beneficial to the environment because they promote
efficient generation and use of power. In addition, by promoting execution of policies directed at
expanding access to energy by rural, poor households, including renewable energy sources, it
promotes less dependence on fossil fuels, and in turn, reduces emissions of carbon dioxide.

149.    Three possible effects on the environment may result from the operation of the thermal
power plant. As noted in Annex 10, these will be examined in greater detail. The first effects
may come from continued reliance on burning traditional fuels, such as wood and charcoal,
which cause indoor air pollution and have impacts on deforestation and land degradation. The
second area concerns the current increase in diesel operated private and industrial generators that
are used to augment electricity in wealthier households, businesses, and industries. Dependence
on these generators is expected to decline with improved electricity generation. However, the
rate and level of substitution will need to be better monitored. Lastly, there is the possibility of
fuel switching when electricity tariffs become less affordable. Some of the switching may result
in increased reliance on biomass fuels that may have consequences for forest ecosystems, etc.
These impacts will be reviewed in the planned PSIA, which will make use of findings from the
Strategic Environmental Assessment (SEA) completed under the Second Environmental
Management and Capacity Building Project (P073089). The policy reforms are also aimed at
improving the institutional capacities for mitigating negative impacts, including technical
advisory support from the Energy Sector Working Group (SWG).

Project

150.    Environmental benefits are expected from investments in energy saving technologies,
such as retooling old equipment to make it more energy efficient or replacing it, and applying
insulation and energy efficient heaters and light bulbs in buildings and industrial areas. The
Project investments for the thermal power plant at the Mutundwe site are expected to have only a
modest impact on the environment. The potential negative impacts identified in the construction
and operation phase of the power plant are mainly: (a) oil wastes in the form of used oil that will
be generated by the plant; (b) emissions; and (c) noise. In addition, there are risks of water
pollution related to oil spills, and occupational hazards including accidents. The Environmental
Impact Assessment (EIA), which was prepared for the Mutundwe Thermal Power Station,
satisfactorily identified all associated adverse impacts and proposes effective mitigation and
monitoring measures.

151.    UETCL will oversee implementation of the findings from the EIA. However, significant
capacity building is needed since there are only a few qualified environmental specialists in
UETCL who can carry out the technical and scientific monitoring. Stakeholder consultations on
the EIA were held with local authorities and village leaders.




                                                41

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                Annex 1


                           Annex 1: Country and Sector Background

                             UGANDA: Power Sector Development

A. Country Background

153.    Uganda, with a per capita income in 2005 of about US$280, is one of the poorest
countries in the world. Life expectancy is low (49 years at birth) while population growth, at 3.5
percent in 2005, is among the highest in the world.

154.    The Government of Uganda (GOU) has demonstrated a firm commitment to poverty
reduction, as spelled out in its PEAP. The most current version aims at key strategic results in
areas of increased GDP growth, reduced poverty and inequality, and improved human
development. IDA's assistance to Uganda is aligned with the strategic direction given in the
PEAP, and the UJAS, approved by IDA and other development partners in January 2006,
provides the basis for their support of the PEAP's implementation.

155.    IDA's and other Development Partners' contributions have brought the country closer to
reaching the Millennium Development Goals of reduced HIV/AIDS prevalence, poverty
reduction, and increased enrollment rates for primary schooling.

156.    The Uganda country program was the first to utilize the Poverty Reduction Support
Credits, which provide external finance to the budget to support the implementation of the
reform agenda derived from the PEAP. Uganda was also the first to benefit from debt relief
under the Heavily Indebted Poor Country Initiative, which resulted in cancellation of 100% of
Uganda's IDA debt on July 1, 2006.

157.    Uganda has experienced robust macro-economic performance in recent years, averaging
6.4% growth between 1990 and 2005. Strong macro-economic policies, a credible program to
eradicate poverty and good financial discipline have led to falling poverty levels. Domestic
inflation has been slightly above the 5 percent target for the third consecutive year due to
inflationary pressures from weather, power shortages and energy price shocks. The Uganda
Shilling (USh) depreciated by 4 percent against the US$ due to higher demand for foreign
exchange to finance the import bill. Overall due to good macroeconomic management, savings,
exports, and foreign direct investment are increasing. The challenge for Uganda is now to deepen
the reforms already underway and prevent their reversal.

B.      Sector Background

Policy Support Program and Project

158.    This section includes: (a) the key characteristics of the power sector; (b) a description of
the Government's comprehensive power sector reform program; and (c) a description of ongoing
and planned IDA-supported power sector operations (see Annex 9 on power sector financial
situation).




                                                43

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                 Annex 1


Key Characteristics of the Power Sector

Hydrology, Supply and Demand

159.   Uganda's main source of power is from the Nalubaale and Kiira 380MW dam complex,
located at the mouth of Lake Victoria. Over the past several years, Lake Victoria water levels
have dropped by about 1.5 meters, and in October 2006 reached levels close to the historic low
of March 1923. However, by March 2007, the lake levels have recovered by about 0.7 meters.
The low water levels have resulted in a decline in electricity output from the Nalubaale/Kiira
from around 200MW in April 2005, dropping gradually to reach 170MW by January 2006,
reducing further to 135MW (equivalent to water discharges of 850m3/s) from February 2006 to
August 20, 2006. Since then, the production has dropped to 120MW (equivalent to water
discharges of 750m3/s). In contrast, current system demand is about 380MW at peak times and
about 290MW at base load, resulting in persistent and acute power shortages which are
impacting growth.

160.   The reasons for these power shortages are fourfold. First, there has been a significant
delay in power infrastructure development in particular, in completing the financing of the
previous Bujagali project, which is the next least-cost generation increment. As part of the
previous effort to develop the project, construction was scheduled to commence in early 2002
and the power station was to be commissioned by the end of 2005. Second, the low Lake
Victoria water levels, caused both by the recent regional drought as well as water over-
abstraction for hydropower generation, have resulted in significantly reduced power generation
output at the Nalubaale/Kiira dam complex. In this regard, the Government has decreased
hydropower production in an effort to return to the principles embodied in the Agreed Curve. A
third contributor to current power shortages has been the high level of technical and non-
technical losses of the distribution system, which are now being addressed by UMEME, the
private sector concessionaire. Fourth, annual demand growth over the past several years
increased by about 8%, placing additional pressure on the power system.

161.   Lake Victoria is a shared transboundary resource of Kenya, Tanzania, and Uganda.
Rwanda and Burundi are a part of the upper watershed that drains into Lake Victoria through
the Kagera River. The lake is part of the Nile River Basin system shared by ten countries:
Burundi, Democratic Republic of Congo, Egypt, Ethiopia, Eritrea, Kenya, Rwanda, Sudan,
Tanzania, and Uganda. In addition to its environmental value, including biodiversity and the
hydrological cycle, Lake Victoria supports a large fishing industry for export and local
consumption, hydropower production, drinking and irrigation water, lake transport, and tourism.
Because of low water levels, these benefits have been threatened by environmental degradation
manifested in reduced fish stocks, the drying out of fish breeding areas and the loss of livelihood
to many fishing communities; a decline of biodiversity; increased sedimentation and nutrient
loads resulting in eutrophication; the drying out of wetlands and loss of littoral habitat;
increased lake transportation costs, since ports and piers are left hanging on dry land, and water
shortages for shoreline towns and farmers. Hence, the consequences of load shedding and
depletion of Lake Victoria have been widespread, with macro, sectoral and environmental




                                                44

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 1


impacts affecting not only Uganda but the other Riparian States. Efforts to regulate and manage
the activities threatening the lake are clearly insufficient at present, and widespread poverty in
the basin exacerbates environmental stress. Even in its current perilous state, the lake is a
valuable asset supporting the livelihoods of approximately three million people directly; and
indirectly the entire population of the basin estimated recently at 30 million.

162.    On July 26, 2006, the daily water outflow regime of the Nalubaale/Kiira dam complex
was modified so as to optimize hydropower generation. Higher volumes of water are now
discharged during the day and lower volumes at night such that the total volume of water
released during the 24 hour period is the same as if the release was at a constant pace (as was
the case up to July 25, 2006). Hydropower output during the day and night is now at 145MW
and 79MW respectively, rather than 120MW continuous. The operating regime of the 2x50MW
short-term thermal plants is now also optimized during peak hours and during the day.

163.    The power supply situation has slightly improved since the optimization of available
generation capacity started in late July. However, the overall load shedding for the calendar
year 2006 remained high with 364 GWh of load shed in 2006. In fact load shedding in 2006
increased by over 370% compared to 2005 (98 GWh of load shed). Those numbers show the
dramatic consequences of the overall capacity constraints in the system that GOU has now
started to address.

164.    The generation mix has changed from a predominantly hydro based system until mid-
2005 to a hydro/thermal mix of 55/45 today and the share of thermal generation is expected to
increase further when the additional 50MW temporary thermal plant, to be financed under the
Project, is commissioned later this year.

165.    In spite of a very low electrification rate, the cost of load shedding to the economy is
significant. The lack of reliable and available power supply and/or the need to run expensive
back up generation has impacted on industrial production (many firms have limited back-up
generation capacity). The cost of unserved energy has been estimated at US$ 0.389 per kWh.
Furthermore, the shortfall in generation has a knock-on effect on the rest of the power sector,
since lower volumes in supply lead to lower retail electricity sales and revenues, which is
affecting the viability of UMEME, the private distribution concessionaire.




                                                  45

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                                   Annex 1

Distribution Performance

169.     The operational performance of UMEME has been satisfactory so far. UMEME's
financial performance is also satisfactory, with healthy cash flows. The main features of its
operational performance include:

    Distribution losses went down from 38% on takeover to around 34.1% as of December 2006.

    Overall collection rate went up from 80% on takeover to 92% by May 2006. Following the
    tariff increase of 37.5% in June 2006, the collection rate dropped to 85% from June to
    October 2006 and to 82% in November/December 2006, following another tariff increase of
    41% in November 2006.

    A total of 36,000 new customers have been connected during the first twenty-two months of
    UMEME's operations to December 2006, against 22,000 committed under the concession.
    At present there are approximately 300,100 customers (270,000 domestic, 29,000 small
    commercial, 800 medium and large industry, and 300 street lights).

    During the first twenty-two months to December 31, 2006, UMEME invested USh24.7
    billion (US$13.6 million) in the network and operational assets, and it paid USh2.5 billion
    (US$1.4 million) to the Government as reimbursement of costs of arranging the concession.
    Under the concession, UMEME had committed to invest a minimum of US$5 million in the
    first eighteen months of the concession.

170.     GOU and UMEME have now agreed that as long as the power crisis persists and until
UETCL's bulk supply tariff has stabilized (i.e. the bulk supply tariff does not increase by (a)
10% or more of the retail tariff within a twelve month period or (b) by 20% or more of the retail
tariff within a thirty-six months period), actual losses minus 1% and the actual non-collection
rate (of the last quarter preceding any tariff change) will be applied in UMEME's tariff
determination. There is downside protection for UMEME, but the benefits accruing from lower
losses will be shared between UMEME and UETCL (see Annex 9).




                                                                                                  47

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                               Annex 1


171.     Box 3 summarizes the main characteristics and key factors affecting Uganda's power
system:

                             Box 3: Uganda's Power System Characteristics
· Uganda's total energy consumption is around 5 million tons of oil equivalent, of which 90 percent is biomass-
    wood, charcoal and agricultural residues. Per capita energy consumption is about 0.2 tons of oil equivalent,
    which is one of the lowest in the world.

· Uganda has one main hydropower complex, located on the Nile River: Nalubaale (180MW), completed in the
    early 1950s, and Kiira (200MW)1, a dam and powerhouse facility located 0.8 km downstream.

· To address the current power shortages, the Government has procured two 50MW short-term thermal power
    plants, and is planning to procure an additional 50MW of thermal power plant under this Power Sector
    Development Operation. An IPP of 50MW running on HFO is also being procured. Thermal power generation
    represented 23% of total generation output in 2006.

· The power sector generated 1,887 GWh in 2005, which decreased to 1,610 GWh in 2006 due to the power
    crisis. Domestic electricity sales revenues were US$116 million in 2006.

· Uganda has the 23rd highest retail petroleum prices in the world ­ about the fifth highest in Africa. The average
    proportion of Ugandan transport costs attributed to fuel charges is estimated at about 50% (compared to an
    average of 30% for Africa).

· There were about 300,100 consumers connected to the national grid at the end of 2006. Efforts to expand rural
    access in areas far from the grid, as well as extending existing connections outside of UMEME's concession
    area, are being pursued under the Energy for Rural Transformation Project.

                                 Key Factors Affecting the Power System
· A significant delay in power infrastructure development and, in particular, in completing the financing of the
    previous Bujagali project, which is the next least-cost generation increment. As part of the previous effort to
    develop the project, construction was scheduled to commence in early 2002 and the power station was to be
    commissioned by the end of 2005;

· The low Lake Victoria water levels, caused both by the recent regional drought as well as water over-
    abstraction for hydropower generation, have resulted in significantly reduced power generation output at the
    Nalubaale/Kiira dam complex (currently only 120 MW of the 300MW installed capacity can be used);

· The high level of technical losses of the distribution system;

· Annual demand growth which has increased by about 8%; and

· Low access rate to electricity of approximately 5%.


Government's Comprehensive Power Sector Reform Program

172.     In June 1999, the Government approved a comprehensive power sector reform strategy
that focused on improving efficiency through private participation in the sector. In November
1999, the Parliament passed a new Electricity Act which provided the legal and administrative


1The total installed capacity of 200 MW will be reached once unit 4 and 5 are commissioned (expected April 2007).
Currently tested and installed capacity is 120MW thereby bringing the totally installed capacity of Nalubaale/Kiira
to 380MW.




                                                        48

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                             Annex 1


framework for reform and removed the legal monopoly of the Uganda Electricity Board UEB.
In April 2000, the GOU created ERA as an independent regulator. On March 31, 2001, the GOU
unbundled UEB into three separate corporate entities, one each for generation (the Uganda
Electricity Generation Company Ltd. ­ UEGCL), transmission (the Uganda Electricity
Transmission Company Ltd. - UETCL) and distribution/supply (the Uganda Electricity
Distribution Company Ltd. - UEDCL). Subsequent to the unbundling of UEB, the private sector
was granted separate concessions for the management of UEGCL's and UEDCL's assets and
UEB was dissolved in early 2006.

173.    Concession of Power Generation Assets (April 2003). In April 2003, Eskom Uganda (a
subsidiary of Eskom Enterprises (Pty) Ltd, the state-owned utility of South Africa) was awarded
a 20 year concession for the management of UEGCL's assets, which consisted of the Nalubaale
and Kiira hydropower plants. UEGCL's function has since been limited to monitoring the
activities of Eskom Uganda. UEGCL has a staff of eleven people and its administration costs are
recovered through a concession fee charged to Eskom Uganda, which in turn, recovers its cost
through its charges to UETCL for the sale of power. Eskom Uganda's charges to UETCL cover
depreciation of capital, the concessionaire's return, annual operation and maintenance costs, and
regulatory fees.

174.    Concession of Distribution Assets (March 2005).         The Government conducted an
international competitive bidding process and awarded a 20-year concession for UEDCL's
distribution assets to Umeme Ltd. (UMEME), a private company established in Uganda in which
Globeleq Ltd. of Bermuda (Globeleq) and Eskom2 had a 56% and 44% ownership, respectively.
Globeleq is a wholly-owned subsidiary of Globeleq Ltd., (UK) which, in turn, is a wholly-owned
subsidiary of Commonwealth Development Corporation of the UK. UMEME is the first
unbundled electricity distribution network in Sub-Saharan Africa to be concessioned to the
private sector. From the time of the handover of operations of the distribution network,
UEDCL's function has been to monitor the activities of UMEME. UEDCL has staff of fourteen
people and its administration costs are recovered through a lease payment charged to UMEME
which UMEME recovers through the electricity retail tariff. The figure below illustrates the
current industry structure.

Electricity Sector Regulatory Framework

175.    The Ministry of Energy and Mineral development (MEMD) oversees Uganda's power
sector. Its key functions include to: (a) provide policy guidance in the development and
exploitation of Uganda's energy and mineral resources; (b) acquire, process and interpret
technical data in order to establish the energy and mineral resource potential of the country;
(c) create an enabling environment in order to attract investment in the development, provision
and utilization of energy and mineral resources; and (d) inspect, regulate, monitor and evaluate
activities of private companies in the energy and mineral sectors so that the resources are
developed, exploited and used on a rational and sustainable basis.

176.    With the approval of the Electricity Act (1999), the legal and administrative framework
for the electricity sector was created to regulate the generation, transmission, distribution, sale,
export and import of electricity in Uganda. Some of the key elements of the Electricity Act are:
        Establishment of ERA as an independent body, specifying its structure, appointment of
        its members, functions, power and administrative duties;
        Creation of an application and implementation procedure for new projects, which is to be
        conducted in an open and public manner, and which allows for both unsolicited proposals
        to be presented to ERA and for ERA to invite applications for a particular license;
        Access to the transmission system to all licensees on a non-discriminatory basis, upon
        payment of the relevant fees and charges as approved by ERA;
        Universal access to the distribution network to all existing and potential users, upon
        payment of the relevant fees and charges as approved by ERA, subject to technical
        constraints;
        Creation of dispute mechanisms; and




                                                             50

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                 Annex 1


        Commitment by the Government to promote and support rural electrification programs,
        including through the creation of a rural electrification fund, which has been established
        and is managed by the MEMD.

The Electricity Regulatory Authority (ERA)

177.    ERA commenced its functions in April 2000. ERA is composed of five members
(including a Chief Executive Officer) appointed by the MEMD, with the approval of the Cabinet.
ERA members are appointed for 5-year terms and can hold a maximum of two terms. ERA has a
total of twenty five permanent staff, including fifteen professionals.

178.    ERA key functions and responsibilities include the following:
        Issue licenses for generation, transmission, distribution and sale of electricity; licenses
        shall remain in force for a maximum period of 40 years and cannot be transferred without
        the written consent of ERA;
        Ensure compliance with the licenses issued and the Electricity Act and, in doing so,
        protect the interest of consumers in respect of electricity tariffs, and quality, efficiency,
        continuity and reliability of electricity supply;
        Establish the sector tariff structure, approve electricity tariffs, and investigate tariff
        charges. The methodology for tariff calculation (which should be determined so that it
        covers all reasonable costs and provides a reasonable rate of return) must be approved by
        ERA and stated in the license;
        Develop and enforce performance standards for the electricity sector;
        Provide the procedure for investment programs by the transmission and distribution
        companies;
        Appoint the system operator, which must be also a transmission licensee (currently
        UETCL); and
        Designate a bulk supplier, responsible for the transmission and sale of electricity in bulk
        to distribution and sales companies (currently UETCL), under the terms specified in the
        license issued by ERA.

179.    Although the Electricity Act allows direct funding for ERA from the GOU and
Parliament, ERA has not received any direct government financing, nor does it rely on
borrowings. Its primary funding (around 70% of its revenues) is through annual license fees,
followed by permit fees and funds from development partners, such as IDA and Nordic
Development Agency.

180.    ERA has played an important role in the oversight of the power utilities operating in
Uganda. It has regulated electricity tariffs effectively since 2001. Since early 2005, the power
crisis brought about by dry hydrological conditions in the region has created significant
challenges for the Government, ERA and the power utilities. ERA has implemented substantial
tariff increases in recent months, 37.5% in June 2006 and 41% in November 2006, in order to




                                                   51

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 1


ensure the financial viability of the power sector. The power utilities now operate at arm's
length. UMEME has achieved satisfactory operational improvements, although some of the
efficiency gains have been reversed to some extent following the large increases in tariffs.

The Rural Electrification Agency

181.    The Rural Electricity Agency was established in 2003 by the MEMD as a semi-
autonomous agency.        The Rural Electrification Agency is mandated to facilitate the
Government's goal of achieving a rural electrification rate of at least 10% by the year 2012 from
1% at the beginning of this decade. The Rural Electrification Agency derives its revenues from a
5% levy applied on UETCL's bulk energy purchase costs. A Rural Electrification Fund was
created to support the Rural Electrification Agency's activities. The Rural Electrification Fund
was designed to partially subsidize initial capital costs and debt financing and provide financial
incentives for private companies to bring electricity to unserved areas.

182.    The Government's strategy in rural electrification is being supported by IDA through the
implementation (in partnership with Global Environment Fund of the Energy for Rural
Transformation Program (Credit 3588-UG). One of the projects currently benefiting from this
program's efforts is the West Nile Rural Electrification Company, a small off-grid project, which
currently supplies electricity during 18 to 24 hours per day to around 1,500 rural clients in the
West Nile area. The company is constructing a 3.5 MW mini-hydro plant and is currently
operating a 1.5 MW diesel power plant, benefiting from a US$7.5 million subsidy under the IDA
financed Credit 3588-UG.

Ongoing and Planned IDA-supported Power Sector Operations

183.    IDA has been involved in the power sector in Uganda for over 20 years through
development of several projects, beginning in 1980 with emergency repairs to the Owen Falls
Dam ­ now called Nalubaale dam (financed by the United Kingdom) which, along with the
Owen Falls Extension (now called Kiira) is a complex on the Nile River constructed and
extended over a period of about 50 years. Other projects include the Power II Project in 1985 for
the rehabilitation the Owen Falls (Nalubaale) Dam, Power III in 1991 for the construction of the
Owen Falls Extension (Kiira), a Supplemental Credit to Power III in 2000; the Power IV Project
in 2001, which has financed Unit 4 and Unit 5 (each of 40MW) at the Kiira powerhouse, and the
Energy for Rural Transformation Project in 2001, which aims to expand rural access to
electricity.

184.    Ongoing power projects financed by IDA are described below (see Annex 2 for a
complete list of donor supported projects in the Uganda power sector).


    · Power IV Project (Credit 3565-UG): US$62 million IDA credit to commission
        additional capacity (80MW) at Kiira; geothermal investigations (shallow drilling), and
        US$12 million for urgent distribution investments, petroleum reform and capacity
        building.




                                                52

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                              Annex 1


   · Energy for Rural Transformation Project (Credit 3588-UG): US$46.5 million IDA
       credit which aims to establish the institutional and legal framework for rural
       electrification and a Rural Electrification Fund, to facilitate scale-up of rural access
       which would otherwise not be a commercial proposition. The project supports the
       development of small and medium-scale renewable energy options, including both grid-
       connected and off-grid mini and micro-hydropower, bagasse based cogeneration plants,
       and biomass gasification.

   · Privatization and Utility Sector Reform Project (Credit 3411-UG): This project was
       amended to include a PRG guarantee mechanism to provide risk mitigation support to
       UMEME, Uganda's private distribution concessionaire. IDA is providing limited risk
       coverage (up to US$5.5 million) to backstop a Liquidity Facility that could be drawn
       upon in the event of non-payment by the GOU of its electricity bills and because of
       failure of ERA to approve tariff adjustments according to the pre-agreed tariff
       methodology set forth in UMEME's distribution and supply license. MIGA is as well
       involved in that project through a Guarantee that covers a substantial part of the
       concessionaire's investments.

185.   In parallel to this Operation, the World Bank Group is also preparing another power
sector related operation, the Private Power Generation (Bujagali) Project. IDA is also supporting
regional power interconnection within the East Africa Community, which would benefit all
countries involved by diversifying supply, reducing investment costs, and increasing electricity
supply.
