Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                 Annex 4



                           Annex 4: Detailed Operation Description

                             UGANDA: Power Sector Development

A.      Operation Development Objective and Key Indicators

Policy Support Program and Project

186.    The primary objectives of the Operation are to reduce short-term power shortages and
financial imbalances, and facilitate orderly longer-term expansion of electricity service.

187.    In order to achieve these objectives, the Operation comprises: (a) a set of investments and
policy measures designed to reduce the supply-demand gap until the Bujagali hydropower plant
comes into service in 2011; (b) financial support for the Government to absorb a part of the high
costs of short-term thermal power generation; and (c) policy measures aimed at making the
sector financially viable, accelerating rural electrification, and moving towards a sector-wide
approach for sector development.

188.    Key performance indicators for the Operation are:

    · Level of power shortages;
    · Financial viability of key sectoral utilities; and
    · Functioning of the Energy Sector Working Group.

B.      The Operation

Policy Support Program

189.    The proposed Policy Support Program of US$80 million will be disbursed on
effectiveness provided that the agreed conditions of disbursement have been met.

190.    This Operation covers three policy areas, with support from the investments under the
investment Project.

Policy Area 1 ­ Power Shortages

191.    There has been considerable activity on the supply side. On the demand side, while there
have been some activities to support energy efficiency measures, the policy framework for such
activities is weak. The measures to be undertaken as part of this operation are:

        · Preparation of an energy efficiency strategy and plan.

Policy Area 2 ­ Sector Finances

192.    As discussed earlier, thermal power generation and high oil prices have created a
financial crisis in the power sector.

193.    The measures to be undertaken as part of this operation are:


                                                 59

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                 Annex 4


       · Tariff increases to partially cover the high cost of thermal power;
       · Government will pay the arrears owed to UETCL, and remain current in the future;
       · UETCL will remain current on its payments to generation companies.

Policy Area 3 ­ Longer-term Sector Expansion

194.   While Uganda has already successfully implemented power sector institutional reforms,
there remains a need to improve some aspects of sector governance. First, there is a need to
improve coordination between the Government and development partners, so as to increase aid
effectiveness and harmonization. Second, there is a need to assess the rural electrification
framework and enhance the effectiveness of the related institutions, which would increase the
likelihood of meeting rural electrification targets. Finally, even though the power sector is not the
sole cause of decline in the level of water in Lake Victoria, it is clear that the power sector must
take steps to ensure that sustainable uses of the water are maintained and enhanced.

195.   The measures to be undertaken as part of this operation are:

    · A joint GOU/Development Partners Energy Sector Working Group will be established
       and become functional with a secretariat;
    · Independent evaluation of the Rural Electrification Agency with subsequent legal
       modifications to its statutes by new legislation if required;
    · Monitoring on a daily basis of water abstraction from Lake Victoria for power
       generation, and reporting to EAC of these data, along with the amounts permitted by the
       Agreed curve;
    · Participation in development of Regional and in-country Watershed Management Plan
       related to Lake Victoria

196.   Conditions. The Policy Support Program will be disbursed after the following conditions
are met:

    Power Shortages

    · MEMD has prepared a draft Energy Efficiency Strategy and Plan ;

    Sector Finances

    · Tariff increases to cover part of the high cost of thermal power (already enacted in 2006);
    · GOU to clear all dues to UETCL;
    · UETCL to clear all dues to generation companies.

    Longer-term Sector Development

    · GOU commitment to undertake independent evaluation (under TOR acceptable to the
       Bank) of the rural electrification framework and institutions, in preparation for "scale-up"
       of rural electrification;
    · Agreement to monitor, on a daily basis, the abstraction of Lake Victoria water for power
       generation as well as amounts permitted by Agreed Curve, and report monthly to EAC;



                                                 60

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                  Annex 4


    · Participation, in Lake Victoria watershed management plans discussions (already
        ongoing);
    · Joint GOU/Development Partners Energy Sector Working Group set up with an agreed
        mandate.

Project

197.    Component 1: Thermal Energy. The Project will co-finance capacity and energy or
kWh charges towards thermal power generation costs payable by UETCL for capacity and
energy charges of a new 50MW short-term thermal plant to be installed at Mutundwe, Kampala
(for the entire contract period). This plant will be fuelled by ADO.

198.    Depending on the final allocation of the financing amount, this subcomponent might also
be used for some kWh imports from Kenya.

199.    Component 2: Technical Assistance and Energy Efficiency (EE). The Project will
support the following activities under this component:

    (a) Energy Efficiency (EE) and Demand Side Management (DSM), to be implemented by
        MEMD and focused on creating an implementation framework for a large-scale program.
        This will entail significant sensitization and capacity building within MEMD and the
        consulting community, as well as sensitization of electricity consumers. A "learning by
        doing" approach is planned, whereby MEMD will expand its ongoing energy audit
        program, and high-impact investments identified by the audit, mostly in the public sector,
        will be financed. This will be complemented by a sensitization campaign, which will
        build on the ongoing work under the Bank-supported ERT. The work also will be
        coordinated with efforts under other Bank projects (e.g., Power IV) as well as activities
        funded by other donors (e.g., GTZ). It will be supplemented by the Swedish
        Development Agency (Sida) with an additional amount of SEK45 million (approx.
        US$6.5 million).

        Under this activity, financed by the Bank, the following sub-components will be
        undertaken:

        (i)     Energy Audits and TA ­ This subcomponent will develop a series of targeted
                energy audits encompassing the public facilities and industries (public or private)
                with the highest social impact and electricity savings potential. This effort will
                leverage work already developed (or under development) by MEMD/GTZ (about
                30 audits in the last three years). MEMD has selected a list of about 30-35
                facilities, including government owned buildings, universities and schools,
                hospitals, water and sanitation, some specific industries (e.g., cement, breweries,
                steel, chemicals) and service companies (e.g., hotels). The audits and additional
                engineering support will be performed in three clusters in order to achieve critical
                mass. The clusters will include: (1) hospitals; (2) public buildings, universities,
                schools; and (3) industries and commercial buildings, including hotels.. Within
                each of the clusters there will be an initial study (e.g., Mulago Hospital, Makerere




                                                  61

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                             Annex 4


          University and National Water and Sewerage Company), training and capacity
          building, and subsequently scaling-up.

    (ii)   Investments in EE and DSM. The objective is to make selective investments in
          investments in EE and DSM initiatives. Target candidates and level of
          investments will be fine tuned based on the results of the above energy audits.
          Allocation of resources will include: (1) co-financing (60-100 percent) investment
          in public facilities, encompassing water and sewerage utilities, hospitals, schools
          and universities and government owned and occupied buildings ­ with the
          objectives of reducing the electricity bills in social and government sectors as well
          as improving the quality of service (e.g., lighting) in schools; and (2) co-financing
          (10-20 percent) of selected private industries with high energy savings and/or
          peak demand saving potential, which should also have a positive demonstration
          effect. Empirical evidence in Uganda has shown significant savings potential,
           with typical financial pay-back periods of 12 months for investments in energy
           efficiency. From an economic standpoint, the pay-back is faster, since the short
           run marginal cost of diesel power generation (about US$20-25/MWh, is almost
           twice as much as the average tariff currently charged to those target customers.
           Tentative allocation of resources, to be refined by MEMD, include the following
           elements:

                   Schools and Universities
                   Water and Sewerage Utilities
                   Hospitals and Clinics
                   Public Owned and Occupied Buildings
                   Private Industry and Service Companies
                   Energy Systems in Police Buildings
                   Kampala City Council Street Lighting

     (iii) Studies, Capacity Building and Technical Assistance

           Studies

          · Energy Efficiency Strategy and Implementation Plan. The draft document
               prepared by MEMD would be reviewed and expanded. Some initial
               suggestions to this draft have been provided by the World Bank. Additional
               aspects to be addressed include, inter alia, incentives and tariffs; institutional
               arrangements; role of UETCL as a single buyer; draft of EE and DSM
               legislation (e.g., new Energy Efficiency Law) and regulations; costs, benefits,
               and priorities of EE and DSM measures; ESCO development; identification
               and elimination of barriers for EE; and a detailed implementation plan with
               milestones and responsibilities.

           · Load Management. Support would be provided for the design and
               implementation of measures for improved load management both on the
               supply and on the demand side. On the supply side, the options include
               incentives to cogeneration, distributed generation and possible "merchant"



                                            62

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                              Annex 4


                 plants. Alternative load shedding schemes will be looked at, in order to
                 allocate energy to the most productive uses. On the demand side, options such
                 as load shifting and alternative tariff schemes (e.g., two-part tariff, critical
                 time pricing) should be looked at. Possibilities to pilot alternatives schemes
                 such as self-rationing need to be discussed, including customer surveys,
                 region to be ring-fenced, monitoring and evaluation and detailed design on
                 scale up. A preliminary analysis is being prepared by a consultant, who has
                 identified the pre-requisites to implement a two-part tariff scheme such as the
                 one used in Brazil in 2001. These include reduction in commercial losses and
                 the system's ability to meet peak demand without load shedding. Those
                 conditions are not present now, but the idea of a two-part tariff should be
                 examined when conditions permit, in order to conserve expensive fuel.

             · Institutional and Organizational Arrangements would be put in place to
                 enable the GOU to apply appropriate standards, codes, labeling, and testing
                 activities for energy efficient appliances, equipment, buildings etc, and to
                 provide capacity building, to MEMD, UNBS and other relevant agencies.
                 Support for the drafting and/or refinement of the Energy Efficiency law
                 regarding these issues would also be provided.

             Capacity Building

             · Capacity Development at MEMD. This includes courses, seminars, experts,
                 visits to other EE and DSM institutions, formal training, and enhancement of
                 project management and procurement capability (either mainstreamed at
                 MEMD or by leveraging existing PIUs, such as those for ERT and Power IV).

             · EE and DSM Training. This training would be for industry practitioners,
                 contractors, consultants and other involved parties, to develop local capacity
                 and assist in implementation of the conservation program in Uganda,
                 including training of trainers to help disseminate knowledge of DSM and EE.

             · Support to the Universities to enhance curricula on EE and DSM.

      (iv)   Communication and Educational Campaigns. This activity encompasses design
             and implementation of tailored programs and mass campaigns to convey
             messages on why and how to save energy, including training and dissemination.
             Customer awareness and education about energy savings will be coordinated with
             UMEME. Media and content will be determined as appropriate. This activity will
             also cover the expenses for the communication strategy for the Private Power
             Generation (Bujagali) Project, whose initial costs are being covered by the Fourth
             Power Project.

  (b) Independent Evaluation of the Rural Electrification Program. This program was
      fundamentally restructured as part of the broader sectoral restructuring in the late 1990s.
      The review will focus on lessons learned to date and adjustment of the program as
      required to proceed to the "scale-up" phase (implemented by MEMD).



                                               63

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 4


   (c) Poverty and Social Impact Analysis. This will examine the distributional impacts of
       energy sector policy reforms on the well-being or welfare of various stakeholder groups,
       in particular, poor and vulnerable populations (implemented by MEMD).

   (d) Power Sector Investment Plan (PSIP). In order to address the challenges facing the
       energy sector, GOU has established an Energy Working Group to ensure that there is
       coherence with Uganda's energy sector strategy, priorities, investment programs and
       budgetary allocations to the sector. GOU proposes to undertake a study to prepare a
       Power Sector Investment Plan which will build on the existing generation, transmission,
       and distribution system planning work already undertaken by relevant sector actors, in
       order to: (i) mobilize private investment, maximize the allocation of limited donor
       resources, form strategic public-private partnerships, and effectively use Government
       budgetary resources in a coordinated manner; and (ii) develop a comprehensive
       monitoring and evaluation system in order to assess the impact outcomes of policies,
       investments and other activities in the energy sector. The PSIP will translate the strategic
       sector plans into a series of carefully-costed and actionable projects or activities over a
       period of up to 10-15 years. The PSIP will include clearly stated long-term objectives and
       corresponding measurable and time-bound targets; specific infrastructure outputs;
       estimated investment requirements; an assessment of the policy and institutional reforms
       in the energy sector that will also be needed to reach the objectives; and an assessment of
       country readiness and implementation capacity for significant scale-up efforts, as well as
       proposed actions to remedy any shortcomings. MEMD will undertake this work in
       coordination with UETCL, UMEME, REA, and the Energy Sector Working Group. The
       work will be performed to international standards of analysis and rigor such that the
       resulting plan will be suitable for donor support. This component will include training of
       suitable staff so that the PSIP can be updated in future.

   (e) Lake Victoria Transmission Ring Pre-feasibility and Feasibility Studies. These studies
       will   assess    the   proposed   (Masaka-Uganda)-(Mwanza-Tanzania)        interconnection.
       Depending on the outcome of the feasibility studies, the Project will also finance the
       detailed design and preparation of tender documents, including the required EIA
       (implemented by UETCL).

   (f) Kalagala Offset Tourism Development Plan. This activity supports the Government's
       plan to institute mitigation measures at the Kalagala Itanda site on the Nile River, below
       Bujagali Falls, to offset the loss of the falls when the contemplated dam is constructed.
       This will include revising the 2001 Tourism Development Concept and Plan, and also
       will provide implementation funds.

   (g) Energy Sector Communications Strategy. This activity will support Government's efforts
       to ensure that key information on sectoral strategy and initiatives is made publicly
       available in a timely and effective manner. The program will encompass MEMD's broad
       energy agenda, and will focus on key messages around energy conservation, rural
       electrification, petroleum development, etc.

200.   The EE and DSM initiatives being proposed as part of this project are complementary
and fully aligned with the ongoing efforts being carried out under the ERT Project. The latter has



                                                64

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                 Annex 4


been focusing on fast-track, high pay-back energy efficient initiatives, by providing efficient
household and street lighting; it also includes a sub-component on power factor correction,
whereby capacitors will be installed at some critical points of the network. Those actions will
enable savings of about 10MW (average) and 40MW during peak hours. The current Project, on
the other hand, focuses on public buildings and large users, with high savings potential. It has a
major component for energy efficiency capacity building in the Ugandan market. Finally, it will
develop some specific programs and methodologies that can be used to mitigate power crises
through better load-shedding or self-rationing schemes.

201.    All these initiatives, whether under ERT or the current project, are meant to be "Win-
Win" interventions, in the sense that they reduce the bill for the customer and benefit the system
as a whole, since the price of energy charged to those customers is about half of the short run
marginal cost (driven by the most expensive diesel fired power plant). In the case of public
facilities that are not able to pay their electricity bills on time, a reduction in consumption helps
to improve the financial position of UMEME because there is less that goes uncollected.

Technical Assistance for Regional Transmission Integration (Total US$2.0 million by IDA)

202.    The study will cover both the pre-feasibility and feasibility studies for the proposed Lake
Victoria transmission ring (Masaka-Uganda)-(Mwanza-Tanzania), and depending on the
outcome of the feasibility study, will also include the detailed design and preparation of tender
documents, including the ESIA.

203.    Low water levels in Lake Victoria have had an impact not only on Uganda's hydropower
production but also on the upstream countries, especially Kenya and Tanzania. A strong regional
electricity grid could have reduced the Uganda's dependency on the hydropower facilities by
providing an alternative source of power in form of imports from either Kenya or Tanzania. This
could also have been cheaper for Uganda considering the associated fuel transportation costs.
The interconnection study will complement other ongoing initiatives under the East African
Community and the Nile Basin Initiative, as outlined below, aimed at regional grid
interconnection. The component will also contribute towards the African Action Plan objective
of regional infrastructure development and integration.

204.    In an effort to widen and deepen regional economic and political cooperation, the
governments of Kenya, Tanzania, and Uganda signed in 2000 a treaty re-establishing the East
African Community (EAC). Article 101 of the treaty (East African Community 2000) states that
the Partner States shall in particular promote within the community:

    (a) The least cost development and transmission of electric power, efficient exploration and
        exploitation of fossil fuels and utilization of new and renewable energy sources;
    (b) The joint planning, training and research in, and the exchange of information on the
        exploitation, development and utilization of available energy resources;
    (c) The development of integrated policy on rural electrification;
    (d) The development of Inter-Partner State electrical grid interconnections; and
    (e) The construction of oil and gas pipelines.




                                                   65

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 4


205.    The East African Master Plan Study, completed in 2005, had the overall objective of
determining whether further interconnection of the power systems of Uganda, Kenya and
Tanzania is technically feasible and economically viable as growth in demand occurs over the
next 20 years. The interconnection infrastructure will form an integral part of a Regional Power
Master Plan aiming for a mutually beneficial development of the three Partner States' power
systems. Other objectives include:

    (a) Preparation of a least cost expansion plan for the region taking into account
        interconnections;
    (b) Determination of relative merits of interconnection options (the mutual benefits that
        accrue from such a development); and
    (c) Determination of the costs associated with an interconnection together with the definition
        of mitigative measures with respect to any environmental issues.

206.    The Study presents details of the generation and transmission system expansion planning
for the region, provides the results of the economic analysis indicating the possible benefits that
could be obtained by increasing the interchanges of electricity amongst Partner States, outlines
the investment costs necessary to implement the recommended system expansion plan, and
provides risk analysis and long run marginal costs as well as plan justification. When the Plan is
implemented the people of East Africa will be assured of sufficient and reliable power supply to
a greater extent, at reasonably affordable tariffs.

207.    The main infrastructure features of the Plan include the creation of the East African
Power Grid through interconnection of national Grids. An East African Power Pool will also be
established, meaning that there will be an automatic sharing of power generation, distribution
and management of power trade and technical functions.

208.    The Study recommends an integrated generation expansion sequence, with Kenya
importing Tanzania's gas generated electricity and Uganda's hydro generation. The Study also
makes the following conclusions in favour of an integrated planning scenario:

    (a) A full integration of the power sector makes sound economic sense;
    (b) Has a net present value benefit estimated at US$500 million compared to each country
        developing its own resources;
    (c) Facilitates development of large scale, least cost, renewable hydro;
    (d) Increases usage of indigenous resources, particularly natural gas, and promotes more
        exploration throughout East Africa;
    (e) Takes advantage of hydro complementarity, reduces hydrology risk and increases energy
        output;
    (f) Reduces dependence on imported hydrocarbons;
    (g) Is cost effective and Kyoto friendly; and
    (h) Is a good foundation for economic partnerships among EAC States.

209.    The Permanent Secretaries responsible for Energy Affairs met on February 4, 2005 to
review the Study outcome and determine the way forward. Among the recommendations of the
Permanent Secretaries were: (a) to fast track implementation of the Arusha-Nairobi and Jinja-
Lessos 220 kV transmission lines; and (b) to consider the transmission ring around Lake Victoria



                                                  66

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                            Annex 4


in the medium term, in order to supply loads in the Kagera Region and increase reliability of the
network in the lake zone. These recommendations were approved by 10th Council of Minister's
meeting, which met in August 2005.

210.    To support the proposed regional integration as adopted by the EAC Council of
Ministers, the African Development Bank, under the Nile Basin Initiative, is financing the
feasibility study of recommendation (a) above (Jinja, Uganda)-Lessos, Kenya) while the project
will support the studies related to (b) (Uganda-Tanzania).

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 6


                            Annex 6: Implementation Arrangements

                            UGANDA: Power Sector Development

Policy Support Program

211.    This is a policy based lending component, and is therefore not directly "implemented."
However, a number of actions will be needed to fulfill the release conditions for the two tranches
of the component. MEMD has responsibility for ensuring that these conditions are fulfilled. This
will require close coordination with MOFED, UETCL, DWD, and other actors such as the
Energy Sector Working Group.

Project

212.    This will be implemented in two parts, according to the mandates and responsibilities of
the sectoral structure which Uganda has adopted. Specifically, UETCL, as the sole-purchaser of
electricity in Uganda, is responsible for entering into Power Purchase Agreements (PPAs) with
generation entities, including the short-term power plant that is the focus of this project. Thus,
UETCL will implement Component 1 (Thermal Energy) in its entirety. UETCL's existing
structure already includes a Thermal Power Project Manager. This Project Manager is
responsible for leading the procurement process, with participation from MEMD and ERA. The
Project Manager and his team monitor performance of power suppliers under the PPAs,
including power quality and quantum of power delivered per billing period. The Project Manager
also initiates payments, which are processed through UETCL's existing accounting and financial
management structures.

213.    MEMD will undertake the Sector Investment Plan. This work will also be done in
coordination with UETCL, UMEME, REA, and the Energy Sector Working Group. The plan is
expected to be suitable for donor support.

214.    UETCL will be responsible for the regional integration study under Component 2. The
interconnection study is designed as an integral element of the broader East African Power Pool
(EAPP) planning process. For this reason, the UETCL Planning unit will coordinate this study
with other planned and ongoing work towards realization of the EAPP.

215.    In keeping with its mandate to promote energy sector policy, MEMD will implement the
Energy Efficiency and Demand Side Management elements of Component 2 of the project. This
is a natural extension and deepening of work that MEMD has undertaken over the past ten years.
This work will be undertaken under the direction of the Assistant Commissioner for Energy
Efficiency. MEMD will recruit additional staff as needed to ensure satisfactory project
performance.

216.    MEMD also has the mandate for broader sectoral oversight, and will therefore take
responsibility for the review of the rural electrification program, and other TA. The Ministry of
Finance will be responsible for the Poverty and Social Impact Assessment.




                                                 69

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                          Annex 7




              Annex 7: Financial Management and Disbursement Arrangements

                              UGANDA: Power Sector Development

Policy Support Program

Fiduciary Aspects

217.     Foreign Exchange System. The safeguards assessment of the Bank of Uganda (BoU),
which was completed by the IMF on April 13, 2003, identified several strengths of the BoU, in
particular with regard to external and internal audit. Vulnerabilities were noted: (a) delays in
completion of the financial statements; (b) internal and external audit findings not reported to the
Board; and (c) non-existence of an audit committee. The BoU authorities have been
implementing measures proposed in the safeguards assessment to address these key weaknesses.
An Audit Committee (of the Board) has been appointed and procedures have been put in place to
ensure that both internal and external audit findings/recommendations, as well as follow-up
actions taken, are appropriately communicated to the Governor and the Audit Committee of the
Board.

218.     Budget Resource Management System. The 2006 PFM performance report, based on the
Public Expenditure and Financial Accountability (PEFA) measurement framework covering the
period 2002/2003­2004/2005, notes marked improvements in the reporting of financial
information in Uganda. It describes improvements achieved in transparency and availability of
actual expenditure data, quality and timeliness of annual financial reports, and periodic
reconciliation. Progress is also noted on the Country Integrated Fiduciary Assessment (CIFA,
2004) action plan, particularly the rollout of the Integrated Financial Management System
(IFMS), and activities under the Public Procurement and Disposal of Assets Authority (PPDA).
Risk associated with lack of clear rules and regulations has been reduced through appropriate
legislation and regulatory frameworks.1 Over the years, fiduciary risks related to poor budget
formulation and budget preparation processes have been reduced and progress will continue to
be guided by the Public Expenditure Management Committee (PEMCOM) to coordinate PFM
reforms now encapsulated in the Financial Management and Accountability Program (FINMAP).
The Government Financial Statements are now being prepared in accordance with the
International Public Sector Accounting Standards (IPSAS) framework. The timeliness and
completeness of reporting has improved while training and capacity building for staff in central
and local governments both at technical and professional levels has continued and has seen a
significant increase in the number of professionally qualified staff. The Auditor General has been
provided access to the accounts on classified expenditures since FY2004.




1   The Public Finance and Accountability Act (PFAA), 2003; Financial Regulations and Treasury Accounting
Instructions.




                                                    70

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                   Annex 7



Disbursement and Auditing

219.    The Credit amount is proposed to be disbursed immediately upon effectiveness; it will
follow IDA's simplified disbursement procedures for development policy lending and will be
disbursed against meeting conditions agreed with Government and not tied to any specific
purchases or subject to procurement requirements. Once the Credit becomes effective and agreed
conditions are met, IDA will disburse the proceeds into an account designated by GOU that is
part of the country's foreign exchange reserves accounts, acceptable to the Bank. The Borrower
shall ensure that upon deposit of the credit into the said account, the foreign exchange amount
will immediately be converted to Uganda shillings and credited to the Uganda Government
Consolidated Fund Account, to be used subsequently for budgeted public expenditures activities
of MEMD. The Borrower will acknowledge receipt to IDA of the amount deposited in the
foreign currency account and credited in local currency to the Consolidated Fund Account. If,
after deposit in the foreign reserve account, the proceeds of the credit or any part thereof are used
for ineligible purposes, (i.e., to finance goods or services on the standard negative list) as defined
in the Financing Agreement, IDA will require the Borrower to either: (a) return that amount to
the foreign reserve account for use for eligible purposes; or (b) refund the amount directly to
IDA. Amounts refunded to IDA upon such request shall be cancelled. The administration of this
Credit will be the responsibility of the MEMD in liaison with the Ministry of Finance.

220.    The following summarizes the fiduciary assurance requirements for receipt of the Credit:

    (a) Foreign Reserve Account. The GOU will acknowledge receipt to IDA of the money into
        the foreign reserve account and crediting of this amount in local currency to the GOU
        Consolidated Fund Account. While no audit is required, it is expected that confirmation
        of receipt will be signed off by both the Accountant General and the Auditor General.
        IDA reserves the right to request an audit of the account as provided for in the financing
        agreement;

    (b) Public (Government) Accounts. The Auditor General is required by law to produce
        his/her annual report to Parliament on the public accounts within nine months of the
        financial year-end. IDA will have access to those accounts; and

    (c) Bank of Uganda. The annual entity financial statements of the Bank of Uganda, audited
        in accordance with international auditing standards as promulgated by the International
        Federation of Accountants, are publicly available.

Project

Country Issues

221.    In recent years, there have been significant improvements in public sector accounting and
reporting in Uganda. Most notably, for more than five years, the annual public accounts of
Government have been produced within the statutory period of four months after the end of the
financial year, and the audits have been conducted in a timely manner.




                                                  71

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                  Annex 7



222.   IDA's 2004 Country Financial Accountability Assessment (CFAA) shows that the GOU
made substantial progress in improving its Public Financial Management systems since the
previous CFAA, which took place in 2001. The fiduciary risks associated with poor budget
formulation and budget preparation processes have reduced. In terms of appropriate legislation
and regulatory frameworks, significant progress has been made to ensure that the risks associated
with lack of clear rules and regulations have been reduced. Further, more useful information is
provided in the annual accounts. However, it is recognized however that the process of
implementing new rules and ways of working does take time, and does require changes in
attitude, continued capacity building and widespread demand for greater accountability.

223.   Risks remain in terms of:

    · Enforcement of procurement and payroll rules and procedures;
    · Completeness of data on debt;
    · Effective independent oversight, and
    · Timeliness and effectiveness of legislative and public scrutiny.

224.   In addition, the appropriate legislative framework for integrity is still being developed.
There remain significant legal, institutional and capacity constraints on the ability of the integrity
bodies (IGG, DEI, Police, DPP) to carry out their various functions including public education,
detection, investigation and prosecution of offenders.

225.   With the support of a number of donor assisted initiatives, the Government is seeking to
rapidly enhance the financial accountability framework in order to: (a) mitigate fiduciary risk in
public expenditure management; (b) achieve economy, efficiency and effectiveness in the use of
public funds; (c) enhance transparency and accountability; (d) enhance staff capacity in public
financial management; and (e) establish an appropriate enabling environment for private sector
development and regulation. These initiatives and reforms have been supported and strengthened
through the Bank's Poverty Reduction Support Credit programs.

Project Risk Assessment and Mitigation

226.   The objectives of the Project's financial management system are to:

    · Ensure that funds are used only for their intended purposes in an efficient and economical
       way;
    · Ensure that funds are properly managed and flow smoothly, adequately, regularly and
       predictably;
    · Enable the preparation of accurate and timely financial reports;
    · Enable project management to monitor the efficient implementation of the Project; and
    · Safeguard the Project assets and resources.

227.   Furthermore, the following are necessary features of a strong financial management
system:




                                                72

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                       Annex 7



   · MEMD and UETCL should have an adequate number and mix of skilled and experienced
        staff;
   · The internal control system should ensure the conduct of an orderly and efficient
        payment and procurement process, and proper recording and safeguarding of assets and
        resources;
   · The accounting system should support the Project's requests for funding and meet its
        reporting obligations to fund providers, including Government of Uganda, IDA, other
        donors, and local communities;
   · The system should be capable of providing financial data to measure performance linked
        to the output of the Project; and
   · An independent, qualified auditor should be appointed to review the Project's financial
        statements and internal controls.

Strengths and Weaknesses of the Financial Management System

230.   The Project financial management is strengthened by the following features:

   · MEMD and UETCL have adequately qualified and experienced accounting personnel,
       most of whom have been trained in World Bank Financial Management and
       Disbursement Guidelines;
   · MEMD and UETCL have budgeting arrangements in place but MEMD has to improve its
       monitoring of its budget, so that variances between actual and budgeted amounts are
       explained, and steps are undertaken to correct adverse variances;
   · External auditing arrangements for both MEMD and UETCL are adequate;
   · MEMD and UETCL both have an Internal Audit unit that has qualified and experienced
       Internal Auditors but experience has shown that they do not get involved in reviewing the
       internal control systems of the project. Hence there will be a need to ensure that the
       Internal Audit units in both MEMD and UETCL carry out this function;
   · Funds flow arrangements are adequate for both MEMD and UETCL;
   · Financial Reporting requirements are adequate for both MEMD and UETCL but
       management needs to ensure that the financial reports are submitted to the Bank in time,
       and Financial Monitoring Report formats are prepared and agreed with the Bank before
       negotiations;
   · MEMD and UETCL both have adequate accounting software but MEMD needs to ensure
       the accounting staff improve on the usage of the accounting software; and
   · MEMD and UETCL have Financial Management Manuals that are sufficient for the
       Project.

Institutional and Implementation Arrangements

231.   MEMD and UETCL will be responsible for the overall implementation of the Project.
MEMD and UETCL are also currently jointly implementing the Fourth Power Project; MEMD is
also implementing the Sustainable Management of Mineral Resources Project.

232.   During Project implementation, MEMD and UETCL will coordinate and manage:

   · Procurement, including purchases of goods, works, and consulting services;
   · Project monitoring, reporting and evaluation;
   · Contractual relationships with IDA and other co-financiers; and
   · Financial management and record keeping, accounts and disbursements.




                                                  76

 Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                              Annex 7


233.    MEMD and UETCL will also constitute the operational link to IDA and the GOU on
matters related to the implementation of the Project.

234.    The Permanent Secretary (PS), MEMD, will be the "Accounting Officer" for the Project,
assuming the overall responsibility for accounting for Project funds. The Managing Director,
UETCL, will report to the Accounting Officer on matters related to accountability for Project
funds that UETCL will manage.

Budgeting Arrangements

235.    The budgeting arrangements of UETCL are adequately defined in its Financial Policies
and Procedures Manual, which will be adopted for the Project. However, the budgeting
arrangements of MEMD concerning the Project are not defined in the Treasury Accounting
Instructions, 2003 and will therefore need to be included in an addendum to the instructions. The
budgets of UETCL and MEMD in relation to the Project will have to be approved by the steering
committee overseeing the operations of the Project. The capacity of the accounting staff to fulfill
budgeting needs of the Project is adequate, and MEMD's and UETCL's accounting software can
adequately cater for the budgeting arrangements of the Project.

Accounting Arrangements

Books of Accounts and List of Accounting Codes

236.    MEMD and UETCL will maintain books of accounts similar to those for other IDA
funded projects. The books of accounts to be maintained specifically for the Project should be set
up and should include: a Cash Book, ledgers, journal vouchers, fixed asset register and a
contracts register.

237.    The books of accounts will be maintained on a computerized system. A list of account
codes (Chart of Accounts) for the Project should be drawn up. This should match with the
classification of expenditures and sources and application of funds indicated in the Financing
Agreement. The Chart of Accounts should be developed in a way that allows Project costs to be
directly related to specific work activities and outputs of the Project.

238.    Books of Accounts to be used for the Project will be opened and a Chart of Accounts will
be completed in accordance with the requirement in the Financing Agreement of the Project of
maintaining books of accounts for the Project.

Staffing Arrangements

239.    MEMD and UETCL have adequate staff to account for the funds of the project. MEMD
has an accounting unit headed by the Project Accountant, who will be responsible for
maintaining the Books of Accounts and records of the Project component funds. The Project
Accountant will be assisted by an Accountant. UETCL has an accounting unit headed by the
Manager Finance, Accounts & Sales, who will be responsible for maintaining the Books of
Accounts and records of the Project component funds.




                                                 77

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                              Annex 7


240.   Most of the accounting staff dealing with this project in MEMD and UETCL has been
trained in World Bank Financial Management and Disbursement Guidelines. The staff will be
advised of any further training requirements as the need arises.

241.   In order to ensure that the Project is effectively implemented, MEMD and UETCL will
have to ensure that appropriate staffing arrangements are maintained throughout the life of the
Project.

Information Systems

242.   MEMD and UETCL have an adequate information system to account for the the Project
funds. MEMD uses Solomon's accounting software while UETCL uses Sun Systems accounting
software. While the UETCL staff has the skills to use the software to produce the Project's
accounts, MEMD staff need to improve their ability to use their software.

Internal Control & Internal Auditing

Internal Controls and Financial Management Manual

243.   The internal controls (including processes for recording and safeguarding fixed assets)
that will be used for the Project are documented in the Financial Management Manuals of
UETCL and MEMD prepared specifically for the Project, which will supplement the existing
manuals used by the entities. Those existing manuals are the Financial Policies and Procedures
Manual for UETCL and the Treasury Accounting Instructions, 2003 for MEMD, which comply
with the Public Finance and Accountability Act, 2003 of Uganda. The manuals prepared for the
Project are living documents that can be updated to strengthen the internal control system when
the need arises.

244.   The manuals should describe:

    · The accounting system which includes the major transaction cycles of the project;
    · Funds flow processes;
    · The accounting records, supporting documents, computer files and specific accounts in
       the financial statements involved in the processing of transactions;
    · The list of accounting codes used to group transactions (chart of accounts);
    · The accounting processes from the initiation of a transaction to its inclusion in the
       financial statements;
    · Authorization procedures for transactions;
    · The financial reporting process used to prepare the financial statements and financial
       monitoring reports, including significant accounting estimates and disclosures;
    · Financial and accounting policies for the project;
    · Budgeting procedures;
    · Financial forecasting procedures;
    · Procurement and contract administration monitoring procedures;
    · Procedures undertaken for the replenishment of the Special Account; and
    · Auditing arrangements.



                                                78

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                              Annex 7


Internal Audit

245.   While internal audit arrangements in MEMD and UETCL are adequate, staff did not get
involved in carrying out internal audit reviews of the Fourth Power Project. Based on this
experience, they need to improve in this area in regard to the present Project. MEMD and
UETCL both have qualified and experienced Internal Auditors. In addition, UETCL has an Audit
Committee, which is a sub-committee of the Board of Directors. The Manager, Internal Audit
and Security reports to this sub-committee, which provides a level of independence to the
internal audit function in this company. An audit committee in MEMD will eventually be put in
place.

246.   MEMD has one Internal Auditor for the whole ministry while UETCL has a department
headed by the Manager, Internal Audit and Security, who manages eleven staff members, six in
the internal audit section and five in the security section. The internal audit section has one
principal internal auditor, three senior internal auditors and two audit assistants. The security
section, which mainly deals with investigations, has one principal security officer, two senior
security officers, one junior security officer and one clerk.

Funds Flow Arrangements

Bank Accounts

247.   The following bank accounts will each be maintained by MEMD and UETCL for this
project:

   · Special Account: Denominated in US dollars. Disbursements from the IDA Credit will be
       deposited on this account; and
   · Project Account: Denominated in local currency. Transfers from the Special Account (for
       payment of transactions in local currency) will be deposited in this account.

248.   These bank accounts shall be opened at Bank of Uganda (BoU) in accordance with the
additional instructions included in the operation Letter of Disbursement. Initial cash flow
forecasts upon which the advance disbursement will be made from the IDA Credit should also be
prepared by the date of opening the accounts.

249.   The account signatories will have to be documented in the addendum to the Financial
Management Manuals of MEMD and UETCL.

Flow of Funds

250.   Funds flow arrangements for the project through the two above bank accounts are:

   · MEMD and UETCL will each prepare a six monthly cash flow forecast based on the
       work plans for the components they are handling and submit a quarterly Withdrawal
       Application under the Financial Monitoring Report (FMR) method of disbursement to
       IDA within 45 days after the end of the quarter.
   · IDA will make an advance disbursement from the proceeds of the Credit based on the
       cash flow forecast by depositing into Special Accounts of MEMD and UETCL.


                                                 79

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                             Annex 7


   · All expenditures will be paid from MEMD and UETCL head offices in accordance with
       the approval mechanisms documented in their Financial Management Manuals.

Disbursement Arrangements

252.   MEMD and UETCL have effective financial management and accounting systems, which
will facilitate the introduction of Financial Monitoring Report (FMR)-based disbursements. The
FMRs will be submitted for disbursement on a quarterly basis. In compliance with FMR
guidelines, each of the Project's implementing units will: (a) sustain a satisfactory financial
management rating during Project supervision; (b) submit FMRs consistent with the agreed form
and content within 45 days of the end of each reporting period, and (c) submit a Project audit
report by the due date.

253.   Within six months after meeting all conditions for disbursement, MEMD and UETCL
will be expected to submit their six months cash flow forecasts, using the FMR method of
disbursement, to IDA for disbursement. IDA will then deposit funds into the Special Accounts of
MEMD and UETCL and these funds will be used by the Borrower to finance IDA's share of
program expenditures under the proposed Credit.

254.   Replenishment of funds from IDA to the Special Account will be made upon receipt of
the quarterly FMRs, which will contain the 6 monthly cash flow forecast for the subsequent
period. If ineligible expenditures are found to have been made from the Special Accounts, the
Borrower will be obligated to refund the same. If the Special Accounts remain inactive for more




                                                  80

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 7


than six months, the Borrower may be requested to refund to IDA amounts advanced to these
accounts.

255.   IDA will have the right, as reflected in the Financing Agreement, to suspend
disbursement of the funds if reporting requirements are not complied with.

Financial Reporting Arrangements

256.   Formats of the various periodic financial monitoring reports to be generated from the
financial management system will be developed. There will be clear linkages between the
information in these reports and the Chart of Accounts. The financial reports will be designed to
provide quality and timely information to the Project management, implementing agencies, and
various stakeholders monitoring the Project's performance.

257.   The following quarterly Financial Monitoring Reports (FMRs) will be produced by
MEMD and UETCL:

    · Financial Reports:
       o Sources and Uses of Funds;
       o Uses of Funds by Project Activity/Component;
    · Physical Progress (Output Monitoring) Report; and
    · Procurement Report on prior review contracts.

258.   The formats for the FMRs have been defined and agreed, and the Project teams have
demonstrated their capability to produce the FMRs to the Country Financial Management
Specialist.

259.   MEMD and UETCL are eligible to use the report-based disbursement method, provided
that during Project implementation, they both: (a) sustain satisfactory financial management
ratings; (b) submit FMRs consistent with the agreed form and content as explained below; and
(c) submit Project audit reports by the due dates. In order to support report-based disbursement,
each implementing unit is required to submit to the Bank the following information:

    · Financial Monitoring Reports (FMRs);
    · Special Account Activity Statements;
    · Special Account Bank Statements;
    · Summary Statement of Special Account Expenditures for Contracts subject to Prior
       Review; and
    · Summary Statement of Special Account Expenditures not subject to Prior Review.

260.   In addition to the quarterly FMRs, MEMD and UETCL will produce, for analytical and
audit purposes, annual financial statements for their components. UETCL will also produce its
annual entity accounts that will be audited and submitted to the Bank within six months after the
year-end.

261.   The financial statements should be prepared in accordance with International Public
Sector Accounting Standards, which, inter alia, includes the application of the cash basis of



                                               81

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                   Annex 7


recognition of transactions. The IDA Credit Agreement will require the submission of audited
financial statements for the Project to the Bank within six months after the year-end by both
MEMD and UETCL.

262.    The Project Financial Statements will be comprised of:

    (a) A Statement of Sources and Uses of Funds / Cash Receipts and Payments, which
        recognizes all cash receipts, cash payments and cash balances controlled by the entity;
        and separately identifies payments by third parties on behalf of the entity.

    (b) The Accounting Policies Adopted and Explanatory Notes. The explanatory notes should
        be presented in a systematic manner with items on the Statement of Cash Receipts and
        Payments being cross-referenced to any related information in the notes. Examples of this
        information include a summary of fixed assets by category of assets, and a summary of
        SOE Withdrawal Schedule, listing individual withdrawal applications; and

    (c) A Management Assertion that Bank funds have been expended in accordance with the
        intended purposes as specified in the relevant World Bank legal agreement.

263.    Indicative formats of these statements will be developed in accordance with IDA
requirements and agreed with the Country Financial Management Specialist.

Auditing

264.    The Auditor General is primarily responsible for the auditing of all government projects.
If the audit is subcontracted to a firm of private auditors, with the final report being issued by the
Auditor General, IDA funds may be used to pay the cost of the audit. The audits should be done
in accordance with International Standards on Auditing.

265.    The audit report for the Project must be submitted to IDA within six months after the end
of each financial year. In addition, UETCL's entity audited accounts should also be submitted to
IDA within six months after the end of each financial year. Any firm of auditors subcontracted to
carry out the audit should meet IDA's requirements in terms of independence, qualifications and
experience.

266.    MEMD and UETCL have managed a number of IDA projects and none of the projects
has an outstanding audit report. No significant issues have been raised in the most recent project
audit reports managed by MEMD and UETCL.

267.    The arrangements for the external audit of the financial statements of the project should
be communicated to IDA through agreed terms of reference. Appropriate terms of reference for
the external auditor must also be developed, agreed with IDA and external auditor appointed
within six months of Credit effectiveness.

268.    GOU does not have a policy that allows public disclosure of audit findings but Article 41
(1) of the Constitution of Uganda allows the public to access this information. The Access to
Information Bill, No. 7 of 2004 has been drafted to complement the clause in the constitution and
is currently with Parliament. Under the Press and Journalist Act, the media also has access to


                                                 82

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                Annex 7


information such as audit findings. In addition, the public is allowed to attend the Public
Accounts Committee of Parliament when it is addressing audit issues.

submission are shown below.

Conclusion of the Assessment

270.    The project's financial management arrangements satisfy the Bank's minimum
requirements under OP/BP10.02. However, some improvements are needed for the system to
provide, with reasonable assurance, accurate and timely information on the status of the Project,
as required by IDA. The recommended improvements are detailed in the Financial Management
Action Plan.

Supervision Plan

271.    A supervision mission will be conducted at least once every year, based on the risk
assessment of the Project. The mission's objectives will include that of ensuring that strong
financial management systems are maintained for the Project throughout its life. A review will
be carried out regularly to ensure that expenditures incurred by the Project remain eligible for
IDA funding. The Implementation Status Report (ISR) will include a financial management
rating for the component. This will be arrived at by the Country Office Senior Financial
Management Specialist after an appropriate review.

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                Annex 8


                              Annex 8: Procurement Arrangements

                             UGANDA: Power Sector Development

Project only; not required for Policy Support Component

A. General

272.   Procurement Environment. In Uganda, all procurements utilizing public funds are
governed by the Public Procurement and Disposal of Public Assets Act, 2003 (the Procurement
Act). The Act, which is based on the UNCITRAL model law, came into effect on January 17,
2003 and established a regulatory body, the Public Procurement and Disposal of Public Assets
Authority (PPDA). The regulations accompanying the Act were made in September 2003 to
apply to all public procurement and disposal activities of ministries, including defense,
departments and agencies of government. PPDA has issued guidelines and forms for use by
Procuring and Disposing Entities (PDEs) and on July 1, 2005, it published standard bidding
documents which are available on its website.

273.   The 2004 Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR) indicates that the
Procurement Act is a good law, thus far, but highlights a few issues with the law and regulations.
These include:

   (a) The need for harmonization of the procurement systems and practices at the local
       government level with those at the central government level. To resolve the
       harmonization issue, the Local Government (Amendment) Act, 2006 was enacted in
       February 2006, and regulations for local government procurement and disposal of public
       assets have been made;

   (b) The provision for negotiations with the best evaluated bidder, which can be open to
       abuse. It is necessary to negotiate a contract: (i) with selected consultants; and (ii) when
       directly contracting a supplier or contractor, but the procurement regulations are not
       specific on these aspects;

   (c) The lack of restriction on use of the merit point system of evaluation for selection of
       consultants. This evaluation system is widely abused in the evaluation of applicants for
       prequalification and in the evaluation of supply and works contracts to make biased
       decisions;

   (d) The provision for pre-qualifying providers on an annual basis which is used to register
       businesses for purposes of soliciting for quotations. This provision is abused by the
       procuring entities not opening bidding opportunities to all the "pre-qualified" providers;
       and

   (e) The need for an appeals body. This has been marginally addressed through an informal
       arrangement whereby cases against the PPDA, or in which the PPDA had provided
       advice, can be taken to the Inspector General of the Government who should appoint
       experts to assist in their investigation and resolution.




                                                 84

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                 Annex 8


274.    Procurement Guidelines. Procurement for the proposed project will be carried out in
accordance with the World Bank's "Guidelines: Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA
Credits" dated May 2004 (revised in October 2006); "Guidelines: Selection and Employment of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers" dated May 2004 (revised in October 2006); and the
provisions stipulated in the Legal Agreement. For each contract to be financed by the Credit, the
different procurement methods or consultant selection methods, the need for pre-qualification,
estimated costs, prior review requirements, and time frame have been agreed between the
Borrower and the Bank and are outlined in the Procurement Plan. A summary of these is
presented below.

275.    Standard Bid Documents and Manuals. The Bank's Standard Bidding documents (SBD)
will be used for all tenders for procurement of goods under International Competitive Bidding
(ICB). Domestic preference would be used under ICB in accordance with Clause 2.55 and
Appendix 2 of the Bank's Guidelines.

276.    The Bank's SBDs will be used for all ICB and national bidding documents agreed with or
satisfactory to the Bank. Contracts which meet the requirements of paragraph 3.13 and 3.16 of
the Procurement Guidelines may be procured using Build-Operate-Transfer (BOT).

277.    The national standard bidding documents for procurement of goods using National
Competitive Bidding (NCB) will be reviewed to ensure that they are generally consistent with
the Bank's Guidelines and procedures prior to clearance by the Bank. At least the first two such
bidding documents prepared using the national standard bidding documents will be subject to
IDA prior review. The standard documents for the procurement of goods can be used provided
that: (a) the standard documents are used for NCB without pre-qualification only as they do not
incorporate clear provisions on the pre-qualification of bidders; (b) margin of preference is not
applicable; (c) bidders are given a minimum of 30 days to submit bids from the date of
availability of the bidding documents; and (d) the document is revised as per the comments
provided by the Bank. The user guidelines for the SBDs needs substantial revision and are not
considered acceptable for project use. Detailed comments on the SBDs will have to be provided
to make the documents fully consistent with the Bank's Guidelines. The revised document
incorporating the comments will be presented to the Bank before Board Presentation. In the case
of consulting services, the Bank's Standard Request for Proposal (SRFP) and standard time-
based and lump-sum contracts will be used for all assignments involving international
competition. The procurement procedures and SBDs to be used for each procurement method, as
well as model contracts for goods to be procured, will be highlighted in the Project
Implementation Manual (PIM).

278.    Procurement of Works. The SIDA Component of the project will finance energy
efficiency renovations, improvement, and replacements for the following: (i) Mula hospital; (ii)
20 rural referral hospitals; (iii) NWSC; (iii) 20 public buildings; (iv) three universities; and (v)
40 public schools. The value of the individual works contracts shall be estimated to cost
US$1,500,000 and may be procured using ICB method of procurement in accordance with the
procurement plan.

279.    The IDA Component of the project will finance energy efficiency renovations,
improvement and replacements for the following: (i) industries and hotels; (ii) ten police



                                                85

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                                 Annex 8


buildings; 80 public schools and colleges; and (iv) public buildings. The value of the individual
works contracts shall be estimated to cost US$1,700,000 and may be procured using ICB method
of procurement in accordance with the procurement plan.

280.   Procurement of Goods. (See Component 1 below)The project will finance capacity and
energy charges of a new 50 MW leased thermal plant to be awarded on the basis of ICB and two
project vehicles to be awarded on the basis of NCB, following procedures that are consistent
with the Bank's Guidelines, and office equipment to be awarded on the basis of Shopping to be
used for small value contracts in accordance with the procurement plan.

281.   Procurement of Non-consulting Services. Procurement of non-consulting services will
follow procurement procedures similar to those stipulated for the procurement of goods,
depending on their nature. The applicable methods shall include NCB and Shopping.

282.   Selection of Consultants. The SIDA Component of consultancy services to be provided
under the project would include: detailed energy audits (i) in Mulago hospital and 20 referral
hospitals; (ii) at the National Water and Sewerage Corporation (NWSC); (iii) in six universities
and 20 public buildings; (iv) 10 public schools and colleges; and (v) in industries and hotels.

283.   The IDA Component of consultancy services to be provided under the project would
include: (i) drafting the energy efficiency policy; (ii) drafting the energy conservation Act; (iii)
other energy rationalization alternatives; (iv) communication and education; (v) detailed audits in
ten police buildings; and (vi) detailed energy audits in 110 schools and colleges. The preferred
method of selection for consultant services would be the Quality and Cost Based Selection
(QCBS) method. Services for audit and other similar services may be procured under contracts
awarded using the Least Cost Selection method in accordance with the provisions of Clauses 3.1
and 3.6 of the Consultant Guidelines. Consulting assignments costing less than US$100,000 may
be procured by using Selection Based on Consultants Qualifications (CQS). Services for tasks
that meet the requirements of paragraphs 3.8 to 3.11 of the Consultants' Guidelines may be
awarded using the Single Source Selection method. Services for which a team of Consultants are
not required and meet all the requirements set forth in paragraph 5.01 of the Consultant would be
procured under contracts awarded to individual consultants in accordance with the provisions of
paragraph 5.1 through 5.3 of the Consultants Guidelines. Short lists of consultants for services
estimated to cost less than US$200,000 equivalent per contract may be composed entirely of
national consultants in accordance with the provisions of paragraph 2.7 of the Consultant
Guidelines.

284.   Capacity Building and Training to be financed by IDA. These activities will cover energy
sector institutions associated with the project components. Overall strategy focuses on
conducting training, and support for training activities through seminars, workshops and training
in the region and abroad based on individual needs as well as group requirements. All training
and workshops will be carried out on the basis of programs, which shall have been approved by
the Bank on an annual basis, and which shall, inter alia, identify: (a) the training and workshops
envisaged; (b) the personnel to be trained; (c) the institutions which will conduct the training; (d)
the duration of the proposed training; The detailed annual training program outline categories of
training, number of trainees, duration of training, staff months, timing and estimated cost.




                                                 86

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 8


285.    Investment Support ­ Component 1: Thermal Energy. The project will co-finance
capacity and energy or kWh charges towards thermal power generation costs payable by UETCL
for the power output of a new 50 MW short-term thermal plant to be installed at Mutundwe,
Kampala (for the entire contract period). This plant will be fuelled by ADO. In anticipation of
this Credit, the Government initiated the ICB procurement process for this thermal power plant.
Bids have been received and evaluated. As of the end of March 2007, pre-contract discussions
are ongoing. A request for IDA's "No-Objection" on the final contract is anticipated prior to
Board presentation.

286.    Operating Costs. Expenditures made for operational costs such as salary, fuel and
stationery will follow Ugandan Government practices in line with the Public Procurement
Directives or, whenever these Directives do not apply, commercial practices commonly used for
the same.

B. Assessment of UETCL and MEMD capacity to Undertake Procurement

287.    Procurement activities will be carried out by mainstream UETCL and MEMD staff.
UETCL will implement the thermal and regional integration components while MEMD will
implement the Energy Efficiency and Demand Side Management component.

288.    An assessment of the capacity of the Ministry of Energy and Mineral Development
(MEMD), and the Uganda Electricity Transmission Company Ltd (UETCL) to implement
procurement actions for the project has been carried out by Richard Olowo, Bank's Procurement
Specialist between May and July 2006. During the September 2006 mission, an updated
procurement capacity assessment of the UETCL and MEMD were carried out by Helen Kofi, the
Bank's Procurement Accredited Staff assigned to the project. The assessment included the
organizational structure for implementing the project and the interaction between the project's
staff responsible for procurement and the Ministry's relevant central unit for administration and
finance.

289.    Based on the assessment, both MEMD and UETCL are in compliance with the
procurement law, the organization of their procurement units, internal technical and
administrative controls, anti-corruption measures, and their appeal mechanisms for bidders are
all rated satisfactory. Further, the compliance with action plans by both agencies is rated
satisfactory from the experience with actions taken on past recommendations under the Fourth
Power Project. Many of the staff who will implement the proposed project has undertaken
procurement training, some on procurement management under Bank financing and in some
cases more than once.

290.    Further, based on the assessment, both MEMD and UETCL have experience in
implementing Bank-financed project procurement, and consistent with the recommendations of
the 2006 ex-post procurement review of the Fourth Power Project, the overall procurement risk
for the proposed project is rated AVERAGE, and mitigation measures are being proposed
below.




                                              87

Uganda Power Sector Development Operation                                               Annex 8


Action Plan

291.    The key issues and risks concerning procurement for implementation of the project have
been identified. To maintain performance at the overall procurement risk rating of average or
better; the following actions are recommended:

a) The project manager, project coordinator, and ERT project procurement specialist and other
   key staff undertake relevant procurement courses in GIMPA in 2007 and/or ESAMI in 2007
   and the UETCL senior procurement officer undertakes a refresher procurement course in
   2007.

b) MEMD establishes a satisfactory procurement record keeping system and designates a
   qualified member of staff to carry out the task within the first three months of Project
   effectiveness.

c) ERT project procurement specialist is deployed to support MEMD senior procurement
   specialist in the project procurement activities on ongoing basis.

d) UETCL acquires the necessary tools and equipment for the project staff and procurement and
   disposal unit to perform the function efficiently during the first three months of Project
   effectiveness.

e) UETCL Financial Policies and Procedures Manual are, within three months of Project
   effectiveness, revised to make it consistent with the procurement law.

C. Procurement Plan

292.    The Borrower, at appraisal, developed a procurement plan for project implementation
which provides the basis for the procurement methods. This plan was discussed during project
negotiations and accepted by the Bank after making the necessary refinements. The procurement
plan shall be updated by the project in agreement with the Bank annually or as required to reflect
the actual project implementation needs and improvements in institutional capacity and be
disclosed after Bank's approval on the updated Plan. The plan will include relevant information
on supply and installation, goods, works and consulting services under the Project as well as the
timing of each milestone in the procurement process.

D.      Frequency of Procurement Supervision

293.    In addition to the prior review to be carried out by the Bank Country Office in Uganda, at
least two supervision missions per year will be undertaken to carry out post review of 20% of all
contracts below the prior review threshold; plus regular sampling of contracts for ex-post review
during supervision mission.

B. Cost of Unserved Energy (CUE)

301.    Power Planning Associates Ltd. took the weighted-average CUE of residential and non-
residential CUE values, with the weighting undertaken on the basis of the imputed split of
unserved demand in 2005. For residential customers, the willingness-to-pay of newly-connected
customers is estimated, in each year, by fitting an income-compensated demand curve between
two points on the curve (see Figure 5 below); the average tariff and average consumption are
used to establish the low price/high quantity point on the curve, and the cost and energy quantum
of a representative mix of electricity substitutes are used to establish the high price/low
consumption point. For non-residential customers, CUE is based on the cost of self-generation.
The Appendix E of the PPA Economic Report contains a 15 page explanation of the many
factors entering into the CUE calculations for residential and non-residential customers.

                    Figure 5: Representation of Residential Energy Value

                                    D
                             P


                                           Value of Substitution



                                                 Value of Induced Demand
                            P1
                                                             Q=A+B*lnP

                            P2

                                                                         D'
                            P2

                                                                      Q
                                        Q1          Q2




302.    It is assumed that the cost of a planned outage in the residential sector is equivalent to the
foregone consumer surplus, which in turn is the amount that the consumer would have been
willing to pay for each unit of electricity as consumption increases. This can be based on the
customer's willingness-to-pay.

303.    The estimation of WTP for newly-connected customers based on this approach is detailed
in 11 pages of Appendix E.1 of the PPA Economic Report. In sum, a WTP value of US$ 0.418
per kWh was calculated for 2005. WTP values are calculated for each year, in response to real
changes in tariffs and fuel prices.

304.    For the commercial and industrial sectors, the results of Power Planning Associates'
research indicate the following costs of self-generation:

305.    The average profile of auto-generation units can be represented by 10% @ 50kVA, 15%
@ 200 kVA and 75% @ 500 kVA. This produces an average unit size similar to the 405 kVA
observed in ERA research.

because it is being used in this context as an alternative cost relative to grid energy, and not as
evidence of willingness to pay. For measuring such social opportunity cost taxes are excluded,
while for evidence of consumer willingness to pay taxes are included.
